The Question 'Where' and Divine Nearness
Explains the question 'where' and divine nearness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God is not subject to spatial questions and how He is described with nearness in a non-spatial sense.

Core Idea
God is beyond place, but His nearness is real—in help, mercy, and guidance.

The second question the passage addresses is 'where is God?' Imam Maturidi explains that God is exalted above place, yet He is described with nearness in ways that relate to His actions and attributes. This lesson clarifies how to understand divine nearness without spatial implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'ayn
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the firm negation of spatial attributes for God.
The passage rejects connection, disconnection, indwelling, and exiting in a spatial sense.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason is that God existed alone before creation, so He cannot change or be spatially related to things.
3
Learn the permissible meanings of nearness
The passage lists several ways God is described as 'near': by help, honor, mercy, guidance. These are real attributes of His essence, not spatial closeness.
Nearness here is relational, not physical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و [ السؤال عن ] الكيفية يحتمل وجهين: أحدهما طلب المثال له، أن يكون مثلاً لشيء من الأشياء ؛ والله واحد يجل عن الأشباه. و [ثانيهما] يحتمل : كيف صفته ؟ فجوابه : مثل الأول أن ليس لصفته كيف، إذ هو طلب المثال ، وهو يتعالى عن الشبه بالذات والصفة؛ إلا أن يريد به أيوصف هو ؟ قيل: بلى، بما وصف به نفسه من الرحمة والعلم والقدرة. وقول القائل : أين هو ؟ سؤال عن مكان، وقد بيّنا أنه يتعالى عن ذلك. ولا يوصف الله سبحانه بالاتصال بالأشياء ولا الانفصال، ولا بالحلول فيها ولا بالخروج منها من جهة المسافة على ما هو ؛ لأن الله تعالى كان ولا غيره، فمحال انتقاله مما كان عليه بكون غيره لما مر بيانه. وعلى التفسير بالخروج من صفات الخلق وشبهه يجوز. ويوصف بالقرب من طريق العون والنصر، ومن جهة التشريف والتخصيص، ومن جهة الرحمة والإحسان، ومن جهة التوفيق والإرشاد، و [ من جهة ] هذا النوع؛ لأن وصف هذا كله وصف ذاتي. جائز أن يقال : لم يزل رحيما بأوليائه، محبا لهم لوقت كونهم له أولياء، مبغضا لأعدائه على ذلك.
And [the question about] the 'how' (kayfiyya) admits two aspects: one is seeking a likeness for Him, that He be similar to something among things — but God is One, exalted above likenesses. And [the second] admits: 'How is His attribute?' So its answer is like the first, that His attribute has no 'how', because it is seeking a likeness, and He is exalted above similarity in essence and attribute. Unless one means by it 'Is He described?' It is said: Yes, by what He described Himself with: mercy, knowledge, and power. And the statement of the questioner: 'Where is He?' is a question about place, and we have explained that He is exalted above that. And God, glorified, is not described by connection with things nor disconnection, nor by indwelling in them nor by exiting from them in terms of distance as it is; because God existed and nothing else existed, so it is impossible for Him to change from what He was by the existence of something else, as has been explained. And according to the interpretation of 'exit' from the attributes of creation and their likeness, it is permissible. And He is described with nearness (qurb) by way of help and victory, and in terms of honor and specification, and in terms of mercy and beneficence, and in terms of success and guidance, and [in terms of] this type; because the description of all this is a description of the essence. It is permissible to say: He has always been merciful to His friends, loving them at the time they become His friends, hating His enemies accordingly.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage addresses two types of questions about God: 'how' (kayf) and 'where' (ayn). For 'how', the author explains that it has two meanings: either asking for a likeness (which is impossible for God) or asking about the nature of His attributes (which also have no 'how' because they are unlike created attributes). The only permissible way to describe God is by what He has described Himself. For 'where', the author states that God is exalted above place; He is not connected or disconnected from things, nor does He dwell in them or exit them in a spatial sense. However, He is described with nearness (qurb) in terms of help, honor, mercy, and guidance—these are real descriptions of His essence. This nearness is not spatial but relational.

Key Terms
الأين
قرب
اتصال
انفصال
حلول
خروج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's nearness to creation is physical or spatial.
Correction: God's nearness is relational—through help, mercy, and guidance—not physical proximity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a way God is described with nearness?
A) Help and victory
B) Honor and specification
C) Physical closeness
D) Mercy and beneficence

Key Takeaway
God is beyond place, but His nearness is real and understood through His actions of help, mercy, and guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt spiritually distant from God and imagined that distance as a physical separation? How does this lesson's teaching on non-spatial nearness challenge that feeling? What steps can you take today to experience His nearness through help, guidance, and mercy?
?
The passage says God's nearness is 'a description of the essence'—real and not metaphorical. If you truly believed that God is near to you in victory, honor, mercy, and guidance, what would change about how you face your current struggles? Will you ask Him for that nearness right now?
?
You often ask 'Where is God?' in moments of distress. But this lesson redirects the question to 'How is God near?' In what specific areas of your life are you failing to see His nearness as help and guidance? What will you do to remove the barriers—like sin or heedlessness—that prevent you from feeling that nearness?

Divine Transcendence
beginner
5 min
Allah's Independence Prevents Description by Created Attributes
Maturidi argues from divine independence against describing Allah by created attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's self-sufficiency (ghani) precludes Him from being described by attributes that originate from His creation.

Core Idea
Allah's independence means He cannot receive praise or benefit from anything He creates.

Maturidi begins by establishing a key principle: if Allah were described by something that comes from another, He would be dependent on that other. But Allah is completely independent (ghani). This short yet profound passage sets the foundation for understanding divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noticing the logical flow: if an attribute comes through another, then need; Allah is independent, so He is not described by that.
Pay attention to the word 'ghani' (independent).
2
Identify the main premise
Trace the logic: 'If through another, then need.' This premise is key to the argument.
3
Reflect on divine independence
Ask yourself: why does describing Allah by something from His creation imply need? How does Allah's independence prevent this?
This connects to the broader theme of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيكون له ذلك بغيره، فيصير بخلقه الخلق ممدوحا منتفعا، وهو الغني بنفسه يتعالى عن أن يكون له بأحد مدح أو نفع. فلذلك لا يوصف بذلك، جل جلاله. ثم القول بفعله، فإنه لا يجوز أن يكون مفعوله، لما لا يُعرف ذلك في الشاهد، ولما يوصف به ولا يوصف بغيره، ولما بينا أن الوصف بغيره يوجب الحاجة إليه ويوصف به في الأزل لما بينا من إحالة التغيّر والزوال، ولما لو جاز الوصف بما هو حال في غيره لجاز الوصف بكل شيء من خلقه، وذلك ممتنع. وقد بيّنا هذا فيما تقدم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
So He has that through another, and through His creation the creation becomes praised and benefited, while He is independent by Himself, exalted above that anyone should have praise or benefit from Him. Therefore He is not described by that, exalted is His majesty. Then the statement about His act: indeed it is not permissible that He be His own effect, because that is not known in the witnessed world, and because He is described by it and not described by other than it, and because we have shown that describing by other than it necessitates need for it and that He be described by it in pre-eternity because we have shown the impossibility of change and passing away, and because if describing by what is inhered in another were permissible, then describing by everything of His creation would be permissible, and that is impossible. And we have explained this previously. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof that Allah cannot be described by attributes that are derived from His creation. The author begins by stating that if Allah were to be described by something that comes from another, He would have that attribute through that other, implying need. But Allah is independent (ghani) and exalted above needing praise or benefit from anyone. Therefore He is not described by such things. Then he addresses the claim that Allah might be described by His act (such as being the creator of health or sickness). He argues that it is not permissible for Allah to be described by the result of His act (like being called 'healthy' because He creates health), because that is not known in the witnessed world (i.e., we do not call an agent by the name of its effect), and because describing by that would necessitate describing by everything He creates, which is absurd. He also invokes the principle that describing by something that inheres in another would imply change and passing away in Allah, which is impossible. Thus the conclusion is that Allah is not described by anything that originates in creation.

Key Terms
الغني بنفسه
الوصف بغيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah can be described by attributes that imply He gains benefit from His creation, such as being 'pleased' in a way that adds to Him.
Correction: Allah is completely independent (ghani) and does not need or gain anything from creation. Describing Him with such attributes would imply need, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it impossible for Allah to be described by an attribute that originates from His creation?
A) Because that attribute would not be perfect
B) Because it would imply Allah is in need of His creation
C) Because creation cannot praise Allah
D) Because Allah cannot be described by any attribute

Key Takeaway
Allah's absolute independence (ghani) means He is not described by any attribute that comes from His creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or give charity, do you catch yourself thinking that Allah 'needs' your worship to be pleased? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what does it demand of your intention?
?
Have you ever felt entitled to reward from Allah because of your good deeds? In that feeling, were you attributing to Allah a need for your actions? What must change in your heart so that you worship only because He is worthy, not to gain anything?
?
Consider a time you were disappointed that your dua wasn't answered. Did that disappointment reveal a hidden belief that Allah 'owes' you something? How can this lesson's truth—that Allah is independent and benefits from nothing—reshape your relationship with Him in moments of waiting?

Allah is Not Described by the Results of His Actions
Maturidi refutes describing Allah by His created effects, using four rational arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that Allah cannot be described by the effects of His creation, such as being called 'healthy' because He creates health.

Core Idea
Describing Allah by His creation's effects would lead to absurdities and imply change.

Maturidi now tackles a specific case: if someone argues that Allah can be described by the results of His actions—like being called 'sustainer' because He provides—he refutes it by showing this would require describing Him by everything He creates, which is impossible. He also invokes the principle that describing by what inheres in another implies change.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic and translation for this argument, focusing on Maturidi's reasons against describing Allah by His act's effects.
Notice the multiple reasons introduced by 'لما' (because).
2
Identify the four reasons
List the reasons: (1) Not known in the witnessed world; (2) He is described by the act, not its effect; (3) Describing by other necessitates need and change; (4) Would permit describing by all creation.
You may need to refer to the commentary for clarification.
3
Reflect on the absurdity
Think about why the fourth reason is a reductio ad absurdum: if we allow describing Allah by one effect, we must allow describing by every effect, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيكون له ذلك بغيره، فيصير بخلقه الخلق ممدوحا منتفعا، وهو الغني بنفسه يتعالى عن أن يكون له بأحد مدح أو نفع. فلذلك لا يوصف بذلك، جل جلاله. ثم القول بفعله، فإنه لا يجوز أن يكون مفعوله، لما لا يُعرف ذلك في الشاهد، ولما يوصف به ولا يوصف بغيره، ولما بينا أن الوصف بغيره يوجب الحاجة إليه ويوصف به في الأزل لما بينا من إحالة التغيّر والزوال، ولما لو جاز الوصف بما هو حال في غيره لجاز الوصف بكل شيء من خلقه، وذلك ممتنع. وقد بيّنا هذا فيما تقدم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
So He has that through another, and through His creation the creation becomes praised and benefited, while He is independent by Himself, exalted above that anyone should have praise or benefit from Him. Therefore He is not described by that, exalted is His majesty. Then the statement about His act: indeed it is not permissible that He be His own effect, because that is not known in the witnessed world, and because He is described by it and not described by other than it, and because we have shown that describing by other than it necessitates need for it and that He be described by it in pre-eternity because we have shown the impossibility of change and passing away, and because if describing by what is inhered in another were permissible, then describing by everything of His creation would be permissible, and that is impossible. And we have explained this previously. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof that Allah cannot be described by attributes that are derived from His creation. The author begins by stating that if Allah were to be described by something that comes from another, He would have that attribute through that other, implying need. But Allah is independent (ghani) and exalted above needing praise or benefit from anyone. Therefore He is not described by such things. Then he addresses the claim that Allah might be described by His act (such as being the creator of health or sickness). He argues that it is not permissible for Allah to be described by the result of His act (like being called 'healthy' because He creates health), because that is not known in the witnessed world (i.e., we do not call an agent by the name of its effect), and because describing by that would necessitate describing by everything He creates, which is absurd. He also invokes the principle that describing by something that inheres in another would imply change and passing away in Allah, which is impossible. Thus the conclusion is that Allah is not described by anything that originates in creation.

Key Terms
المفعول
الشاهد
إحالة التغيّر والزوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because Allah creates health, He can be called 'healthy' in a literal sense.
Correction: Health is a created attribute that inheres in bodies. Applying it to Allah would imply change and need, which is impossible. Allah is described by His act of creating, not by the created quality.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it not permissible to describe Allah by the results of His actions?
A) Because the results are created and do not exist eternally
B) Because describing Allah by that would imply He is in need of His creation
C) Because that is not known in the witnessed world and would necessitate describing Him by everything He creates
D) Because the Quran forbids it

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot be described by the effects of His actions, as that would necessitate describing Him by every created thing and imply change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unwittingly described Allah in a way that implies change or need, such as thinking He 'gets angry' after disobedience as if anger comes upon Him? How can this passage correct your heart?
?
When you praise Allah with His names like 'The Creator', do you ever feel a distance between you and Him because His actions seem separate? How does knowing He is not described by His effects draw you closer to Him?
?
If you truly believe that describing Allah by any of His creation is impossible, what does that demand of your tongue when you speak of Him in your daily life? Will you commit to purifying your speech about Allah starting today?

The Universal Agreement on Tawhid and Its True Meaning
Maturidi surveys diverse groups' agreement on a single origin, then defines the true meaning of divine oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how different philosophical and religious groups agree on the concept of a single origin, and how Maturidi defines the true oneness of God in essence and attributes.

Core Idea
All groups agree on one Creator, but true tawhid means absolute uniqueness in essence and attributes.

Maturidi begins by noting a striking fact: despite their many disagreements, materialists, dualists, and religious groups all affirm a single First Principle. This universal agreement points to the innate human recognition of tawhid. But then he explains what true oneness really means—far beyond mere numerical unity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups mentioned
Read the passage carefully and note the three groups: the people of al-dahr (materialists), the dualists, and the people of religions. What does each group affirm about the First Principle?
Notice that they all affirm a single origin, even if they differ on its nature.
2
Understand the agreement
Despite their differences, all groups agree upon 'the One' and that He has no likeness. This agreement shows the innate nature of tawhid.
Maturidi uses this as evidence that tawhid is universally recognized.
3
Grasp the true meaning of the One
Maturidi defines true oneness: one in highness and majesty, one in essence (no likeness), and one in attributes (no partner in knowledge, power, takwīn).
True tawhid excludes any comparison between the temporal and the eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في طرق التوحيد. قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم القول بالتوحيد من طريق آخر هو أن قول أهل الدهر على اختلافهم اتفق على واحد بارئ، أو قدم طينة أو هيولى، وهو واحد حتى اعترضت فيه الأعراض وتغيرت عن الحال الأولى. وقول الثنوية: إن الحكيم الرحيم العليم واحد، وإن معنى الآخر ليس هو بمعنى الربوبية بل هو ضد معناه، إذ هو سفه كله وشر. وأهل الأديان يثبتون القدم للواحد، حتى قال قوم بتجسمه من بعد، وقوم: إن له ابنا. فهم على اختلافهم أجمعوا على الواحد ونحو ذلك وأنه ليس بذي شبيه. إذ محال ذلك؛ إذ لم يكن غيره فهو على ذلك؛ إذ الوجه الذي فيه شبه وجود ما في غيره من الحدث، وذلك بعيد. وهذا معنى الواحد؛ إنه إذ هو واحد في علوه وجلاله، وواحد الذات منزه عن أن يكون له في ذاته مثال؛ إذ ذلك يسقط التوحيد وقد بيناه وواحد الصفات، يتعالى عن أن يشركه أحد في حقائق ما وصف به من العلم والقدرة والتكوين، بل كل وصف من ذلك لغيره به، بعد أن لم يكن، ومحال مماثلة الحديث القديم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: أعطى جميع البشر ممن له نظره التوحيد في الجملة؛ ثم نقض
Question on the ways of tawḥīd. The jurist Abū Manṣūr, may Allāh have mercy on him, said: Then the statement of tawḥīd from another way is that the saying of the people of the age (al-dahr), despite their differences, agreed upon a single Creator, or on the eternity of clay or hyle, and it is one until accidents intervened in it and changed it from its first state. And the saying of the dualists: the Wise, the Merciful, the Knower is one, and the meaning of the other is not in the sense of lordship but is opposite to its meaning, for it is all foolishness and evil. And the people of religions affirm eternity for the One, until some said He became embodied later, and some said He has a son. They, despite their differences, all agreed upon the One and like that and that He has no likeness. For that is impossible; since there was nothing other than Him, He is upon that; indeed the aspect in which there is likeness of existence of what is in other than Him of temporality, and that is far. And this is the meaning of the One: that He is one in His highness and majesty, and one in essence, transcendent from having any likeness in His essence; for that would nullify tawḥīd, and we have explained it, and one in attributes, exalted above anyone sharing with Him in the realities of what He is described with of knowledge, power, and bringing-into-existence (takwīn). Rather, every attribute from that is for other than Him by Him, after it was not, and the comparison of the temporal to the eternal is impossible. And there is no power except with Allah. Abū Manṣūr, may Allāh have mercy on him, said: All of humanity, those who have reflection, were given tawḥīd in general; then they violated it.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the ways of tawḥīd (divine oneness). The author begins by noting that despite their differences, the 'people of the age' (materialists) agreed on a single Creator or on the eternity of some primordial matter (clay or hyle) which is one but then changed by accidents. Similarly, the dualists (al-thunawiyya) affirm that the Wise, Merciful, Knower is one, while the other principle is not divine but evil and ignorance. Then the author mentions that the people of (revealed) religions affirm eternity for the One, though some later said He became embodied or has a son. Despite these differences, all groups agreed on the oneness of the origin and that He has no likeness. The author argues that this agreement is significant because it is impossible for there to be a likeness to God. The meaning of 'the One' is then explained: He is one in His highness and majesty, one in essence, transcendent from any likeness, and one in attributes; no one shares in His attributes of knowledge, power, and takwīn (bringing into existence). Any attribute in another comes from Him after non-existence. Comparing the temporal to the eternal is impossible. The passage ends with the observation that all humanity were given tawḥīd in general, but then they violated it (naqaḍa).

Key Terms
التوحيد
الدهر
الثنوية
الربوبية
التكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the agreement of different groups on a single Creator validates their specific doctrines.
Correction: Maturidi uses the agreement to show that tawḥīd is innate but later corrupted, not to endorse any particular group's doctrine.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does it mean that God is 'one in attributes'?
A) His attributes are multiple but equal.
B) No one shares with Him in the realities of knowledge, power, and takwīn.
C) His attributes are distinct from His essence.
D) He has attributes like human beings but to a greater degree.

Key Takeaway
True tawhid means God is absolutely unique in essence and attributes, with no partner or likeness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that all groups agreed on a single origin, yet they corrupted it. Where in your own understanding of God do you find subtle corruptions—perhaps thinking of Him in human terms, or doubting His absolute uniqueness in some attribute? What will you do this week to purify your belief?
?
Maturidi says God is 'one in attributes, exalted above anyone sharing in the realities of what He is described with of knowledge, power, and bringing-into-existence.' Do you truly live as if God's knowledge is unlike yours, His power unlike any power you see? When you feel self-sufficient or reliant on your own abilities, have you forgotten this lesson? Commit to one moment today where you consciously acknowledge His exclusive attributes.
?
The passage ends by saying all humans were given tawhid in general but then violated it. What violation of tawhid is still present in your heart—perhaps attributing ultimate control to something other than God, or fearing a creation as if it has independent power? What concrete step can you take tomorrow to correct this?

Divine Transcendence

The Correct Definition of Tawhid
Maturidi defines correct tawhid as encompassing essence oneness, transcendence, and specific attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definition of tawhid as God being one in essence, transcendent above numerical attributes, and described by pre-eternity, bringing-into-being, and power.

Core Idea
Tawhid means God is one in essence, not needing change, with attributes of pre-eternity, power, and bringing-into-being.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi presents the correct understanding of tawhid according to the Ahl al-Sunna. He clarifies that God's oneness is not like the oneness of created things; it is a unique oneness that transcends numbers and composite attributes. He then identifies three key attributes that describe God without implying change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without rushing. Then read the translation. Let the words settle before analysis.
Notice the rhythm of negation and affirmation.
2
Understand the negative aspect
Identify what God is not: He is beyond individuals, numbers, change, boundaries, and end. Ask yourself why these negations are necessary.
These negations guard against anthropomorphism.
3
Understand the positive aspect
Find the three attributes Maturidi affirms: pre-eternity (qidam), bringing-into-being (takwin), and power (qudra). Reflect on how these are eternal and not subject to change.
These attributes are key in Maturidi theology.
4
Reflect on the takeaway
Consider why the passage ends with praise. How does this definition of tawhid guide worship?

فحصل قول فريق بالتوحيد أنه واحد الذات إليه حاجات الآحاد متعال عن معنى الآحاد عما يوجب صفة الأعداد ويتمكن فيه التغير والزوال أو الحدود والنهاية موصوف بالقدم والتكوين والقدرة جل وعز عن التغير والزوال والحمد لله على كل حال
So the statement of a group regarding tawhid is established: that He is One of essence, to Him are the needs of individuals, transcendent beyond the meaning of individuals, beyond what necessitates the property of numbers, and in which change, passing away, boundaries, and end can occur; He is described by pre-eternity, bringing-into-being, and power; He is exalted and mighty above change and passing away. And praise be to Allah in every state.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the correct tawhid according to the people of truth. Maturidi affirms that God is one in essence, meaning His essence is unique and indivisible. All individual needs return to Him alone. He is transcendent beyond the concept of individuals or units that would imply composition or number. Change, decay, boundaries, and ends cannot occur in Him. Instead, He is described by three essential attributes: pre-eternity (qidam), bringing-into-being (takwin), and power (qudra). These attributes do not necessitate change. God is exalted above change and passing away. The passage concludes with praise.

القدرة (power)

Correction: The passage shows that oneness does not negate attributes; it only negates attributes that imply multiplicity or change.

A) Pre-eternity, bringing-into-being, and power

D) Mercy, justice, and wisdom

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defines tawhid as affirming God's unique oneness, transcendence from numerical attributes, and three essential attributes: pre-eternity, bringing-into-being, and power.

?

The Absurdity of an Eternal Being Changing Its Inherent State
Maturidi refutes the possibility of an eternal being changing its essential nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that an eternal being cannot change its essential attribute, and the absurd consequences that follow if such change were allowed.

Core Idea
An eternal being can never lose or gain an essential property without ceasing to be eternal.

Maturidi now turns to refute the materialists who claim the world is eternal with fundamental opposites like separation and union. He shows that if an eternal entity could change its inherent state, then the eternal could become temporal and vice versa, leading to logical chaos. This argument establishes that God's attributes are immutable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' at the end.
2
Identify the target view
The passage refutes those who claim pre-eternal entities with endless accidents, and those who distinguish between separation and union as inherent states.
The target is the dahrīyya (materialists).
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Maturidi shows that if an eternal being could change its state, the eternal could become originated and vice versa. This leads to two aspects: the world would be originated, and an entity could be both united and separate simultaneously.
These are the two aspects mentioned in the passage.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The absurd consequences include the inversion of all opposites, which destroys the doctrine of an eternal world (al-dahr). This proves that the world must be originated and that God's attributes are unchangeable.
This conclusion supports divine transcendence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And hyle. C) And to ignorance of both according to the doctrine of pre-eternity, and it resembles that this is the saying of the adherents of natures [according to] that it did not appear [that from their saying]. Then [there exists a saying] of non-separation or non-union and they consider that upon which the world is to be upon both; and upon that is the saying of those who say with the pre-eternity of entities with accidents that have no first. And the saying of the one who distinguishes between the two states is apparent contradiction, because he necessitated one of the two aspects for itself from separation or union; since that is its attribute by pre-eternity; then that departed from it without the departure of itself, so what it was upon became invalid along with the cause by which it was, and that is the existence of a cause for bringing the thing into existence at the moment of its removal; and that is intellectually corrupt. Moreover, if this were permissible, then the eternal could become originated and the originated eternal, and in that is the invalidation of their saying concerning pre-eternity. Moreover, if it were permissible for that which is established by itself to be passing away and that which persists by itself to be permanent, then it would be permissible for that which exists by itself to be non-existent, and for that which is non-existent by itself to be existent. And in that are two aspects. One: the existence of the world after it was not, and its existence after non-existence, and in that is the corruption of their doctrine and the necessity of saying that the world is originated without an origin for it. And there is no power except with Allah. And the second: if it were permissible for the inherently united to become separated and the inherently separated to become united without something occurring to it, then it would be permissible for the united to be separated at the time of its being united, since its essence is subsisting; and that is something the intellect cannot bear, along with what eliminates knowledge of others entirely; since there is no sign more indicative than what I have mentioned. And in that is the permissibility of making evil good, darkness light, the living dead, the moving stationary, the cold hot, and similar opposites; and in the permissibility of that is the invalidation of the doctrine of the pre-eternity of separation and union, since they would be together, and in that is the corruption of the doctrine of time (al-dahr). And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine that the world is eternal (dahrīyya) and specifically of those who claim that fundamental opposites like light and darkness are eternally separated or united. The argument proceeds by showing the absurd consequences of allowing any change in the state of an eternal entity. The author begins by noting that those who are ignorant of both separation and union while affirming pre-eternity are inconsistent. He then examines the view that the world's state is based on pre-eternal entities with endless accidents. The core argument is that if something is eternally characterized by a property (e.g., being separated or united), then any change in that property would imply that the eternal can become temporal and vice versa, which is absurd. This would also allow self-subsisting entities to pass away, leading to contradictions. The author then presents two aspects of this absurdity: first, if the world can exist after non-existence, it must be originated (ḥādith); second, if an inherently united thing can become separate without an external cause, then it could be both united and separate simultaneously, which is intellectually impossible. Such a scenario would erase all knowledge of distinction and permit the inversion of all opposites, thereby demolishing the concept of eternal separation or union and ultimately refuting the doctrine of an eternal world (dahr). The passage is structured as a reductio ad absurdum, systematically showing that the claims of the materialists (dahrīyya) lead to logical and metaphysical chaos.

القديم (the eternal)
الحديث (the originated)
التباين (separation)
الاجتماع (union)
الدهر (time/eternity of the world)

Misconception: The argument is about physical properties like hot/cold, but it is actually a metaphysical argument about the necessary attributes of an eternal being.
Correction: Maturidi uses physical opposites as examples, but the core is the logical impossibility of an eternal being changing its essential attribute.

Key Takeaway
If an eternal being could change its inherent state, all distinctions collapse, proving that God's attributes are immutable and the world must be originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you assume His mercy or justice have changed, adjusting your faith accordingly? This argument demands that you examine whether you treat God as changeable based on your own feelings. Will you repent of that inconsistency today?
?
The argument shows that if an eternal being could change, all moral and physical opposites (evil/good, darkness/light) would become indistinguishable. In your own life, where have you blurred the line between what God has clearly defined as good and evil, perhaps excusing sin as 'just a circumstance'? Are you living as though absolute moral distinctions are real because God is immutable?

The doctrine of an eternal world (dahr) is destroyed by this argument. Yet, many people live as if this world will never end, planning and investing endlessly in it. What is one concrete change you can make to demonstrate that you are a seeker of the eternal Hereafter, not of this temporary world?

Maturidi's Refutation of Ibn Shabib's Principle of al-Aslah
Maturidi refutes the concept of al-aslah and defends divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the idea that God must create only for the most beneficial, and how he upholds divine wisdom without imposing obligations on Allah.

Core Idea
God's wisdom is not bound by expectations of benefit; He is free from any imposed obligation.

In this passage, Maturidi directly challenges Ibn Shabib's claim that God must always act for the greatest benefit. He argues that such a notion would imply an obligation on God, which is incompatible with divine sovereignty. Instead, Maturidi affirms that God's actions are always wise, but not for the sake of benefiting creation in a way that imposes a right against Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi uses concise, logical phrasing.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Maturidi first argues that the question 'why did He create?' is invalid. Then he rejects the claim that God only does the most beneficial, showing it would impose blame on God.
These are the two pillars of his refutation.
3
Understand the logical consequence
If God must act for the most beneficial, then a description of blame would attach to Him if He delayed or advanced. This is unacceptable.
This shows that al-aslah leads to anthropomorphism.
4
Note the conclusion about benefit
Maturidi concludes that creating for benefit is meaningless because if God did not create, there is no harm upon creatures.
This undermines the very basis of Ibn Shabib's argument.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this refutation preserves God's transcendence and free will, while still affirming His wisdom.
This is a key Maturidi position on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الذي يزعم يوجب أنه لا يجوز أن يُجعل أول الخلق غير الممتحن حتى يكون له في الذي ذكرنا، وإذا جاز غيره بلا مصلحة لذلك الوقت دون غيره لا معنى لما قال. وقد بينا نحن القول بالخلق، وإحالة السؤال عن لِمَ خَلَق؟ وليس لنا أن نزعم أنه لا يفعل إلا الأصلح فيلزمه حق الفعل حتى يلحقه وصف ذم إن أخر أو قدم؛ بل الله تعالى ـ إذ هو حكيم ـ لا يخرج فعله عن الحكمة. وأما اعتبار الأصلح لغيره / إنما تقدير الحق عليه لا تقدير الفعل بذاته"؛ ومحال كون الحق لغيره عليه و لا غير، أو السؤال عن جملة الخلق. فالقول في أنه يخلق لنفع لهم أو صلاح لهم لا معنى له؛ إذ ليس عليهم فيما لا يخلق لهم ضرر ولا فساد فيكون الخلق لما ذكر. والله أعلم.
This that he claims necessitates that it is not permissible to make the first creation other than the one tried, so that he has a [benefit] in what we mentioned. And if another is permissible without benefit for that time without other, his statement has no meaning. And we have already stated the doctrine of creation and the invalidity of the question "why did He create?" And it is not for us to claim that He only does the most beneficial, so that a right of action is incumbent upon Him, so that a description of blame would attach to Him if He delayed or advanced. Rather, God—since He is Wise—does not bring His act out of wisdom. As for considering the most beneficial for another, it is only an estimation of right upon Him, not an estimation of the act by itself. And it is impossible that right be upon Him for another, and no other, or the question about the whole of creation. So the statement that He creates for a benefit or good for them has no meaning, because there is no harm or corruption upon them in what He does not create, so that creation would be for what was mentioned. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is Maturidi's critique of Ibn Shabib's view. Maturidi argues that the notion that God must create only for a benefit or obligation is false. He states that we cannot question God's actions with "Why?" because He is sovereign. The concept of 'al-aslah' (the most beneficial) implies a duty on God, which is unacceptable. God is wise, and His actions are always in accordance with wisdom, but not for the sake of benefiting creatures in a way that imposes a right upon Him. Moreover, if God did not create something, there is no harm to creatures, so the idea of creation for benefit is meaningless.

Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies any purpose in creation.
Correction: Maturidi actually denies that purpose imposes obligation on God; he affirms that God's actions are wise, but not for the sake of benefiting creatures in a way that gives them a claim.

Checkpoint

B) Because God is not obligated to benefit creatures.
C) Because creation is an illusion.
D) Because the question is about the whole of creation.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that God is wise and His actions are always in accordance with wisdom, but He is not obligated to create for the benefit of creatures.

Responding to the Question of God's Location: Beyond Place and Indwelling
Maturidi responds to the question of God's location and critiques a faulty answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question of God's location is invalid and how Maturidi corrects a faulty answer that suggests God is in things by management.

Core Idea
God is not in any place; He was without place and remains so.

The question 'Where is God?' assumes that God occupies space, which contradicts His transcendence. Imam Maturidi presents a response that begins with the principle of divine incomparability, then critiques a previous answer that attempted to locate God 'in things' by management. This lesson walks through the correct Islamic position on this fundamental theological issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: a foundational principle, a presented answer, and a correction by the Shaykh.
The passage is dense; take your time to absorb each part.
2
Identify the faulty answer and its critique
Locate the answer that says God is 'in things, managing them.' The Shaykh points out a mistake. Understand why this answer is insufficient: it implies a relationship of containment or preoccupation.
The phrase 'not by indwelling' (la ʿala al-hulul) is key to avoiding pantheism.
3
Grasp the correct answer
The correct response is that God was without place before creation and remains without place. He is not described by locations. The metaphorical 'being in work' only applies to humans.
This upholds divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجواب ذلك عندنا هو الله الواحد الذي ليس كمثله شيء. وبهذا الحرف نقطع سبيل العود إلى السؤال؛ لأنه يعود إلى ما يتصوّر في الوهم وفي هذا نفيه إلا من حيث الوجود بالأدلة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم أجاب عن قوله "أين هو؟": إنه في الأشياء، مدبر لها، لا على الحلول، كما يقال: "فلان في عمله"؛ وقال: لا على إحاطة الأشياء به. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وقد أخطأ في الجواب، بل حقه أن يقال: تسأل عن المكان، وقد كان ولا مكان، وهو يتعالى عن الوصف بالأمكنة، بل هو على ما كان بلا تغير ولا زوال. والقول بالكون في العمل إخبار في المتعارف عن العمل الشاغل له الحابس فيه عن غيره، والله يتعالى عن هذا الوصف.
And the answer to that with us is that God is the One, there is nothing like Him. And with this statement we cut off the path of returning to the question because it returns to what is imagined in the mind, and in this is its negation except from the perspective of existence through proofs. And there is no power except with God. Then he answered regarding His statement 'Where is He?': He is in things, managing them, not by indwelling, as it is said 'So-and-so is in his work'; and he said: not by things encompassing Him. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: He made a mistake in the answer, rather it is proper to say: You ask about place, and He was while there was no place, and He is exalted above description by places, rather He is as He was without change or cessation. And the statement 'being in work' is a customary report about work that occupies one and prevents him from other things, and God is exalted above this description.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a response to a question about God's location. It begins by affirming the principle that God is unique and unlike anything, which cuts off further questioning. Then it presents a previous answer that God is in things managing them, not by indwelling (ḥulūl). The commentator (al-Shaykh) critiques this answer, stating that God existed before place and remains without place. The phrase 'being in work' is metaphorical for human preoccupation and not applicable to God. The correct response is to deny location altogether.

المتعارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'God is in things managing them' means God is literally present in every object like a pantheistic view.
Correction: The passage clarifies 'not by indwelling' and rejects the notion of things encompassing God. It is a metaphorical management without spatial containment.

B) He is everywhere

Key Takeaway
God is not located in any place or thing; He exists without spatial limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
This lesson says the correct answer is to remain silent about 'how' and affirm Allah as He was without place. Are you comfortable with not knowing the 'how' of Allah's existence? What aspect of your own need for control or certainty keeps you from fully accepting divine transcendence?

Negation Requires Affirmation of Meaning
Clarifies that denying spatial attributes of God must affirm the intended meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that negating spatial attributes from God must be grounded in a proper understanding of those attributes, as emphasized by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Denial without understanding is empty: affirmation of meaning precedes negation.

This passage presents a key methodological principle in Islamic theology: before we deny certain attributes of God, we must first affirm and understand what those attributes mean in the created realm. Imam Maturidi explains that simply negating terms like 'place' or 'movement' is insufficient unless we have a clear conception of what is being denied.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the list of terms that Maturidi mentions: place, exiting, entering, connection, separation, etc.
2
Identify the key principle
Maturidi says negation is impermissible without 'affirming the reality of what is expressed' (taḥqīq al-malfūẓ). This is the core idea: you must first understand the meaning before denying it of God.
This principle applies to all spatial and relational attributes.
3
Reflect on why this matters
Consider how this prevents empty or misguided negation. For example, saying 'God is not in a place' is only meaningful if you know what 'place' means. Without that, the negation is incoherent.
This guards against theological errors like those of the Mu'tazila or anthropomorphists.
4
Apply to other attributes
Maturidi extends the principle to gathering, separating, movement, stillness. Think about how the same logic applies to any attribute we negate of God.
This shows the systematic nature of Maturidi's approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذ لم يجز الوصف بالمكان وبالخروج أو الدخول أو الاتصال أو الانفصال أو البينونة أو نحو ذلك على نفي تلك الأحوال، من غير إثبات تحقيق الملفوظ — وكذلك شأن الاجتماع والافتراق والتحرك والسكون - لم يجز الذي قالوه، وبالله التوفيق.
Then, since it is not permissible to describe with place, or with exiting, or entering, or connection, or separation, or distinctness, or the like, upon the negation of those states, without affirming the reality of what is expressed—and likewise the matter of gathering and separating, movement and stillness—what they said is not permissible, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage addresses the proper way to negate spatial and relational attributes from God. Maturidi argues that one cannot simply deny such attributes (e.g., place, motion) without first affirming the true intended meaning in the mind. For example, saying God is not in a place is only valid if we understand what 'place' means and then negate it appropriately. The same applies to other relational concepts like gathering, separating, movement, and stillness. Thus, the approach of those who negate without first affirming the reality of the expression is invalid.

Key Terms
نفي
إثبات
المكان
الملفوظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that simply saying 'God is not in a place' or 'God is not moving' is sufficient for tanzih (transcendence), without needing to understand what those terms mean.
Correction: Maturidi insists that negation must be based on a prior affirmation of the meaning of the term. Without understanding what 'place' means, the negation is meaningless and thus invalid.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi require before negating spatial attributes of God?
A) A proof from the Qur'an
B) Affirming the reality of what is expressed (al-malfūẓ)
C) Total silence
D) Following the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
Affirming the meaning of a term is a prerequisite for correctly negating it from God; denial without understanding is invalid.

Why God Cannot Create a Like of Himself
Maturidi argues that creating a likeness of God is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three rational arguments proving that it is impossible for God to create a being exactly like Himself.

Core Idea
A created being cannot be like the Eternal Creator.

Some might wonder if God's power is limited if He cannot create another being like Himself. Maturidi addresses this by showing that the very concept of a 'like' of God involves contradictions. This passage lays out three logical proofs that such a creation is impossible, affirming divine transcendence without limiting God's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once carefully. Notice the questioning style: 'Then he asked himself...' Follow the flow of the argument.
The text has some uncertainties (indicated by square brackets).
2
Understand the first argument
Identify the first argument: any created being is originated (muhdath), while God is eternal (qadim). Likeness demands equivalence in attributes, but originated and eternal are incompatible.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument: every created thing is either a body (showing signs of being made) or an accident (not self-subsisting). God is neither, so no likeness.
4
Understand the third argument
The third argument: every originated thing is susceptible to annihilation (fana'), while God is not. Therefore, a 'like' would have to be both annihilable and not, which is contradictory.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why these arguments show that the question itself is impossible, not that God lacks power. The impossibility is logical, not a deficiency in divine capability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سأل نفسه: يقدر أن يخلق مثله ؟ قال : ذا محال، لما فيه إيجاب مخلوق، والمخلوق محدث، وهو قديم، فيبطل أن يكون مثله؛ كما به يسأل عن القدرة ، وهو مثل الأول في الإحالة. وأيضا قال في ذلك إثبات مصنوع، وهو لا يخلو من أن يكون جسما فيه آثار صنعه أو عرَضاً لا يقوم بنفسه، ونفسه يدل على حدثه، وهو ليس كواحد منهما . قال : وأيضا إن كل محدث يحتمل الفناء، وهو يتعالى عن احتمال حدوث الفناء لما ما لا يجوز عليه الفناء، يصير ولما لا يجوز في غيره وقت، [ فهو ] على قلب ذلك
Then he asked himself: Can He create a like of Himself? He said: That is impossible, because it would necessitate a created being, and the created is originated, while He is eternal, so the like would be nullified. As with the question about power, it is similar to the first in impossibility. Also, he said in that is the affirmation of a made thing, and it cannot be free from being a body bearing traces of His work, or an accident that does not subsist by itself, and its very self indicates its origination, while He is not like either of them. He said: Also, every originated thing is susceptible to annihilation, while He is exalted above the possibility of the occurrence of annihilation, because that which does not permit annihilation upon it—so it becomes—and because that which does not allow time in other than Him—[it is] upon the opposite of that.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God cannot create a being exactly like Himself. Maturidi presents several arguments: 1) A created being is originated (muhdath) while God is eternal (qadīm), so they cannot be alike. 2) Any created thing is either a body or an accident, both of which show signs of being made and are contingent, while God is neither. 3) Every originated thing is liable to annihilation (fanā'), whereas God is not subject to annihilation. Therefore, the concept of a being exactly like God is logically impossible. The passage ends with a reference to the opposite of that, implying a contradiction in the question itself.

Key Terms
مخلوق
محدث
قديم
فناء
جسم
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power is limited because He cannot create another being like Himself.
Correction: Maturidi shows the impossibility is logical, not a lack of power. It is as absurd as asking God to create a square circle; it is not a failure of power but a contradiction in terms.

Checkpoint

A) Because such a being would be eternal, but created things are originated
B) Because God does not want to
C) Because He lacks the power
D) Because such a being would be a body

Key Takeaway
God cannot create a being exactly like Himself because likeness would require sharing essential attributes (eternity, self-subsistence, necessity) which are unique to God alone.

?

The Impossibility of a Like of God
Maturidi argues against a like of God using origination and annihilation, with shaykh's refutation of evasion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a 'like' of God is logically impossible according to Imam Maturidi's argument based on origination and annihilation.

Core Idea
A 'like' of God would have to be both originated and eternal—a logical contradiction.

Imam Maturidi refutes the claim that God could have a 'like' by exposing a contradiction in the questioner's own premise. The argument hinges on the nature of originated things and their susceptibility to annihilation, which cannot apply to the Eternal. The shaykh then identifies the questioner's evasion, showing that the question itself is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice how the argument builds from the concepts of origination and annihilation.
The passage is dense; take your time to understand each premise.
2
Identify the key premises
Identify the premises: (1) If something is originated, it cannot be eternal. (2) Every originated thing is subject to annihilation. (3) God is neither originated nor subject to annihilation. Therefore, a like of God cannot be originated or subject to annihilation, but the questioner's 'like' would have to be created, hence originated.
The contradiction is that the 'like' would have to be both originated (by being created) and not originated (to be like God).
3
Understand the shaykh's analysis
The shaykh points out that the questioner evades the issue: he asks if God can create a like, but defines that like as something that cannot be a body, accident, originated, or subject to annihilation. Yet 'created' implies originated, so his definition contradicts his question.
This shows that the question is not a genuine request but a rhetorical evasion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محدث، وهو قديم، فيبطل أن يكون مثله؛ كما به يسأل عن القدرة ، وهو مثل الأول في الإحالة. وأيضا قال في ذلك إثبات مصنوع، وهو لا يخلو من أن يكون جسما فيه آثار صنعه أو عرَضاً لا يقوم بنفسه، ونفسه يدل على حدثه، وهو ليس كواحد منهما . قال : وأيضا إن كل محدث يحتمل الفناء، وهو يتعالى عن احتمال حدوث الفناء لما ما لا يجوز عليه الفناء" ، يصير ولما لا يجوز في غيره وقت، [ فهو ] على قلب ذلك. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } ومن تأمل ما ذكر عرف حَيْد السائل في المسؤول" عن سنن القول { فيما له احتمال التمكن في العقول، لأنه سأل : يقدر أن يخلق مثله ؟ ومن / يكون مثله لا يكون جسما ولا عرضاً ولا محدثًا ولا محتملاً للفناء؛ لأنه إن كان على شيء من ذلك فلا يكون مثله، وبه سأل؛ فكيف يبقى ذلك الذي ذكر . وما ذكر هو أبعاض ما في كون ذلك نفيه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Something originated, and it is eternal, thus it is invalid that there be a like of Him; just as by it one asks about power, and it is like the first in impossibility. And also, he said in that an affirmation of a made thing, and it is not free from being a body with traces of its making or an accident not subsistent by itself, and itself indicates its origination, and He is not like either of them. He said: And also, every originated thing is subject to annihilation, and He is exalted above the possibility of the occurrence of annihilation because that which is not permissible to annihilate, it becomes, and what is not permissible for another to have time, [it is] on the opposite of that. The shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: Whoever reflects on what was mentioned knows the evasion of the questioner in the question about the way of the saying, in what has possibility in the intellects, because he asked: Can He create a like of Him? And whoever is like Him is not a body, nor an accident, nor originated, nor subject to annihilation; for if he were anything of that, he would not be like Him, and by that he asked; so how does that which he mentioned remain? And what he mentioned are parts of what is in the negation of that. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument against the possibility of God having a 'like' (mithl), combined with a commentary by the imam (shaykh) identifying the questioner's evasion. The argument begins by noting that if something is originated (muhdath) yet claimed to be eternal (qadīm), it is invalid to be a like of God—since the like would share in the originated nature. The questioner asks about power (qudra), but the object under discussion (the 'like') would have to be either a body with traces of making, or an accident not subsistent by itself, both of which indicate origination (ḥudūth). God is neither. Furthermore, every originated thing is subject to annihilation (fanā'), but God cannot be subject to the possibility of annihilation, so the 'like' would have to be eternal, contradicting its originated nature. The shaykh then explains that the questioner evades the issue: he asks 'Can God create a like?', but the very definition of a 'like' of God is that it cannot be a body, accident, originated, or subject to annihilation. Since the questioner's own words imply the 'like' would be created (hence originated), his question is self-contradictory. The passage concludes that his mention is part of the negation of such a possibility.

Key Terms
مثل (like)
محدث (originated)
قديم (eternal)
فناء (annihilation)
قدرة (power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can create any possible entity, including a being exactly like Himself.
Correction: A 'like' of God is logically impossible because it would have to be both originated (by being created) and eternal (by being like God), which is contradictory. The issue is not limited power but logical impossibility.

Checkpoint

B) It would be originated and subject to annihilation, while God is eternal and not subject to annihilation.
C) It would have power, and God alone has power.
D) The questioner is a heretic.

Key Takeaway
A 'like' of God is impossible because it would require an originated thing to be eternal and not subject to annihilation—an inherent contradiction.

Contradiction in the Question of Creating a Like: Maturidi's Refutation
Maturidi demonstrates the logical contradiction in asking whether God can create a like.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various logical contradictions that arise from the question of whether God can create a like of Himself, as refuted by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Asking if God can create a like is self-contradictory and undermines God's identity.

Maturidi presents a multi-layered argument showing that the question 'Can God create a like?' is internally contradictory and implies absurd conclusions about God's identity and power. This passage is a dense refutation covering principles of divine transcendence and the nature of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation. Notice the repeated phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' as a refrain.
The passage contains several distinct arguments; don't worry about details yet.
2
Identify the first impossibility
Maturidi starts from the principle that God cannot be under power (qudra). Understand why this makes the question impossible: any 'like' would either push likeness away or subordinate the other to Him.
This is based on the school's (Maturidi) own premises.
3
Analyze the contradictory nature of the question
The question 'Can God create a like?' is contradictory because a 'like' of God cannot be created. If it is created, it is a creature, not a like. Recognize the logical trap.
Maturidi uses a linguistic analysis: 'like' and 'created' are incompatible in this context.
4
Examine the identity and divinity arguments
The passage then argues that admitting a like would cause the loss of God's identity (huwiyya) and divinity (uluhiyya), since divinity is based on transcendence above likeness.
Also consider the contradiction of a 'made thing' (maj'ul) being a maker (ja'il).
5
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Finally, Maturidi turns the question back on the Mu'tazila: if God became a creator through power, why can't something else become a creator similarly? This exposes inconsistency in their view.
This step shows how the opponent's own premises lead to an absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الإحالة على مذهبنا سهل، وهو أن الله جل جلاله محال دخوله تحت القدرة، فالقول بإدخال غير تحت القدرة ليصير بها مثله دفع المثلية عنه لإحالة دخوله تحت القدرة، والآخرُ بها يُلحقه به . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وإن شئت قلت : السؤال متناقض، لأنه قال : يقدر أن يخلق مثله؟ ومثله لا يكون مخلوقا . فكأنه قال : يقدر على ما ليس له مثل من الخلق ؟ فبلى. وأيضا إن في احتمال غير [على] ما هو عليه سقوط هويته، فيكون ذلك الغير هو الذي به يكون هوية الأشياء . ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل أن الله سبحانه إنما ثبتت له الإلهية بما حقق تعاليه عن المثل والشيئية، فمحال احتمال مثله لما به سقوط ألوهيته . على أن الكلام متناقض من الوجه الذي يقول ؛ لأنه يخبر أن يُجعل، فصيّره مجعولاً. ومحال كون مجعول جاعل على إزالة الجعل الذي به كان لما به زواله ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم على قولهم كان غير خالق فصار خالقا . فقد أدخله من هذا الوجه تحت القدرة التي بها صار خالقا . فكيف ينكر جواز من لم يكن كذلك فيصير كذلك بأنه خلقه كذلك ، كما صار كذلك بأن خلق غيرا ؟ والله المستعان.
Then the impossibility according to our school is easy: that God, exalted be His glory, is impossible to enter under power, so the statement about bringing another under power so that it becomes a like of Him is a repulsion of 'likeness' from Him due to the impossibility of His entering under power, and the other by it attaches him to Him. And there is no power except with God. And if you wish, I would say: The question is contradictory, because he said: 'Can He create a like of Him?' And a like of Him cannot be created. It is as if he said: 'Can He create what has no like from creation?' Yes. And also, in the possibility of another [being] upon what He is, there is a falling away of His identity, so that other would be that by which the identity of things is constituted. And there is no power except with God. The principle is that God, glorified, only has divinity established by what actualizes His transcendence above like and 'thingness', so it is impossible to admit a like because of the falling away of His divinity. However, the discourse is contradictory from the aspect of what he says, because he informs that it is made, so he made it a made thing. And it is impossible for a made thing to be a maker in removing the making by which it was, because of the removal of that by which it existed. And there is no power except with God. Then according to their saying, he was not a creator and then became a creator. So he brought him under the power by which he became a creator. Then how can he deny the permissibility of one who was not like that and then becomes like that, in that He created him like that, just as he became like that by creating another? And God is the one sought for help.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered refutation of the possibility of God having a like, highlighting contradictions in the question. The author begins by stating that in his school (Maturidism), the impossibility is simple: God cannot be under power (qudra). So any claim that God brings another under power to become a like would either push 'likeness' away from God (since God is not under power) or attach the other to God (making it subordinate). The question itself is contradictory because a 'like' cannot be created (makhluq): if it is created, it is not a like. The author then argues that if another 'like' were possible, it would cause the loss (suqut) of God's identity (huwiyya), because that other would be the basis for the identity of things. The principle is that God's divinity (uluhiyya) is established by His transcendence (ta'ali) above likeness and 'thingness' (shayiyya). Admitting a like would nullify that. Furthermore, the discourse is contradictory because it speaks of the 'like' being made (yuj'al), making it a 'made thing' (maj'ul). A 'made thing' cannot be a maker (ja'il) because removing the 'making' would destroy it. Finally, if Mu'tazila claim that God was non-creator and became creator (through power), then why can't something else become a creator similarly? This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
إحالة (impossibility)
قدرة (power)
مثل (like)
هوية (identity)
إلهية (divinity)
تعال (transcendence)
مجعول (made thing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the question 'Can God create a like?' is a neutral or innocent inquiry about divine power.
Correction: The question is self-contradictory because a 'like' of God cannot be created; it implies absurdities such as the loss of God's identity and divinity.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say about the 'like' if it were made?
A) It would be eternal and uncreated
B) It would become a maker itself
C) It would cause the loss of God's identity
D) It would be subject to annihilation like other creatures

Key Takeaway
The question of God creating a like is logically impossible and undermines God's divinity and identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you ever unconsciously picture a being with human-like qualities? How does Maturidi's argument about the impossibility of a 'like' challenge you to purify your conception of Allah? What will you do today to replace any mental image with pure transcendence?

You often say Allah is the 'Creator of everything,' but do you truly believe that includes the idea that nothing can be equal to Him? Is there an area of your life where you treat something as if it were a 'like' to Allah—giving it ultimate trust, fear, or love? What step will you take to dismantle that idol?
?
Maturidi says the question itself is contradictory. Are there questions you ask about Allah that are actually rooted in a false assumption? How can you train yourself to recognize and abandon such questions, submitting to the fact that Allah's reality is beyond your full comprehension? Commit to one specific change in how you think about Allah starting today.

The Contradiction in Asking God to Create a Like
Maturidi exposes the contradiction in the question 'Can God create a like of Himself?'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question 'Can God create a like of Himself?' is self-contradictory and logically impossible.

Core Idea
A like of God cannot be created, so the question is inherently contradictory.

Imam Maturidi begins by exposing a fundamental flaw in the question of whether God can create a being like Himself. He argues that the very notion of a 'like' implies equality and uncreatedness, making the question self-defeating. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi uses a direct and sharp reasoning style.
2
Identify the contradiction
Find the sentence where Maturidi says the question is contradictory. Understand his logic: if a like is created, it cannot truly be a like.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider what this means for the nature of God: He is unique and beyond comparison.
This aligns with the Islamic concept of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بإدخال غير تحت القدرة ليصير بها مثله دفع المثلية عنه لإحالة دخوله تحت القدرة، والآخر بها يلحقه به. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وإن شئت قلت: السؤال متناقض، لأنه قال: يقدر أن يخلق مثله؟ ومثله لا يكون مخلوقا. فكأنه قال: يقدر على ما ليس له مثل من الخلق؟ فبلى. وأيضا إن في احتمال غير [على] ما هو عليه سقوط هويته، فيكون ذلك الغير هو الذي به يكون هوية الأشياء. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والأصل أن الله سبحانه إنما ثبتت له الإلهية بما حقق تعاليه عن المثل والشيئية، فمحال احتمال مثله لما به سقوط ألوهيته. على أن الكلام متناقض من الوجه الذي يقول؛ لأنه يخبر أن يجعل، فصيره مجعولا. ومحال كون مجعول جاعل على إزالة الجعل الذي به كان لما به زواله. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم على قولهم كان غير خالق فصار خالقا. فقد أدخله من هذا الوجه تحت القدرة التي بها صار خالقا. فكيف ينكر جواز من لم يكن كذلك فيصير كذلك بأنه خلقه كذلك، كما صار كذلك بأن خلق غيرا؟ والله المستعان.
By bringing something under power to make it his like, [that] repels the likeness from him due to the impossibility of it entering under power, and the other [like] is brought near to him by it. And there is no power but in God. And if you wish, you say: The question is contradictory, because he said: Is He able to create his like? And his like cannot be created. So it is as if he said: Is He able to create that which has no like from creation? Yes. And also, if something other than what he is occurs, that causes the loss of his identity, so that other is that by which things have identity. And there is no power but in God. The principle is that God Almighty only possesses divinity because He is verified to be exalted above likeness and thingness, so it is impossible that His like should occur, because it would cause the loss of His divinity. Moreover, the speech is contradictory from the aspect that he says; because he says that it is made, so he makes it something made. And it is impossible for something made to be a maker by removing the act of making by which it existed, because that would cause its removal. And there is no power but in God. Then according to their statement, He was not a creator and then became a creator. He has thus entered under the power by which He became a creator. So how can he deny the possibility of one who was not thus becoming thus because He created him thus, just as He became thus by creating another? And God is the source of help.

Commentary
This passage is an argument demonstrating the impossibility of God creating a being like Himself. The author begins by stating that if something were brought under divine power to become His like, that would negate His uniqueness because the like would have to be under power. The question 'Can God create a like of Himself?' is shown to be self-contradictory: if the like is created, it is not truly a like, because a true like would be uncreated. The argument then proceeds to show that if such a like were possible, it would imply a change in God's identity and divinity. The author also refutes the idea that God could have been non-creator and become creator, which would subject Him to change and power, contradicting divine perfection.

Key Terms
مثل (mithl)
قدرة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God can create anything, including a being like Himself.
Correction: This argument shows that a like of God is logically impossible, not merely beyond power. The concept 'like of God' is incoherent.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the question 'Can God create a like of Himself?' is contradictory?
A) Because God cannot create anything.
B) Because a like of God would have to be uncreated, so the question is self-defeating.
C) Because God's power is limited.
D) Because the question is not asked properly.

Key Takeaway
The question 'Can God create a like of Himself?' is logically incoherent because a like of God cannot be created by definition.

?

?

?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly innocent question can be contradictory. What other questions about God might you be asking that are actually incoherent? Will you commit to examining your own questions with the same rigor?

The Impossibility of a Like Undermining God's Identity and Divinity
Maturidi argues that a like of God would negate His identity and divinity, and refutes the idea of God becoming a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the possibility of a like of God would undermine His unique identity and divinity, and that the idea of God changing from non-creator to creator is also contradictory.

Core Idea
A like of God would negate His divinity, and the notion of God becoming a creator is absurd.

Maturidi continues his argument by showing that if a like of God were possible, it would destroy God's very identity and divinity. He also refutes the objection that God was not a creator and then became one, a view that would imply change in God. Let's explore these deeper points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the principle Maturidi states about God's divinity being based on transcendence.
2
Analyze the identity argument
Understand that if something were to occur that is like God, it would cause the loss of God's identity, because identity is unique.
3
Examine the creator argument
See how Maturidi refutes the idea that God could change from non-creator to creator, which would imply He was subject to power.
This reinforces divine immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بإدخال غير تحت القدرة ليصير بها مثله دفع المثلية عنه لإحالة دخوله تحت القدرة، والآخر بها يلحقه به. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وإن شئت قلت: السؤال متناقض، لأنه قال: يقدر أن يخلق مثله؟ ومثله لا يكون مخلوقا. فكأنه قال: يقدر على ما ليس له مثل من الخلق؟ فبلى. وأيضا إن في احتمال غير [على] ما هو عليه سقوط هويته، فيكون ذلك الغير هو الذي به يكون هوية الأشياء. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والأصل أن الله سبحانه إنما ثبتت له الإلهية بما حقق تعاليه عن المثل والشيئية، فمحال احتمال مثله لما به سقوط ألوهيته. على أن الكلام متناقض من الوجه الذي يقول؛ لأنه يخبر أن يجعل، فصيره مجعولا. ومحال كون مجعول جاعل على إزالة الجعل الذي به كان لما به زواله. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم على قولهم كان غير خالق فصار خالقا. فقد أدخله من هذا الوجه تحت القدرة التي بها صار خالقا. فكيف ينكر جواز من لم يكن كذلك فيصير كذلك بأنه خلقه كذلك، كما صار كذلك بأن خلق غيرا؟ والله المستعان.
By bringing something under power to make it his like, [that] repels the likeness from him due to the impossibility of it entering under power, and the other [like] is brought near to him by it. And there is no power but in God. And if you wish, you say: The question is contradictory, because he said: Is He able to create his like? And his like cannot be created. So it is as if he said: Is He able to create that which has no like from creation? Yes. And also, if something other than what he is occurs, that causes the loss of his identity, so that other is that by which things have identity. And there is no power but in God. The principle is that God Almighty only possesses divinity because He is verified to be exalted above likeness and thingness, so it is impossible that His like should occur, because it would cause the loss of His divinity. Moreover, the speech is contradictory from the aspect that he says; because he says that it is made, so he makes it something made. And it is impossible for something made to be a maker by removing the act of making by which it existed, because that would cause its removal. And there is no power but in God. Then according to their statement, He was not a creator and then became a creator. He has thus entered under the power by which He became a creator. So how can he deny the possibility of one who was not thus becoming thus because He created him thus, just as He became thus by creating another? And God is the source of help.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is an argument demonstrating the impossibility of God creating a being like Himself. The author begins by stating that if something were brought under divine power to become His like, that would negate His uniqueness because the like would have to be under power. The question 'Can God create a like of Himself?' is shown to be self-contradictory: if the like is created, it is not truly a like, because a true like would be uncreated. The argument then proceeds to show that if such a like were possible, it would imply a change in God's identity and divinity. The author also refutes the idea that God could have been non-creator and become creator, which would subject Him to change and power, contradicting divine perfection.

Key Terms
مثل (mithl)
إلهية (ilāhiyya)
تعالٍ (ta'ālī)
هوية (huwiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God could create a being equal to Himself without affecting His divinity.
Correction: Maturidi shows that a like would directly undermine God's unique identity and divinity, as divinity requires absolute uniqueness.

Checkpoint

A) God's power is limited.
B) God's divinity is based on His transcendence above likeness.
C) God does not want a like.
D) A like would be too difficult to create.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of a like of God would destroy His unique identity and divinity, and any notion of change in God is incoherent.
