The Futility of Seeking Causes for God's Actions
Maturidi refutes the search for causes in God's actions and defines the proper human response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that seeking a cause (illa) for God's actions is impossible and that the proper response is worship and obedience.

Core Idea
Seeking causes for God's actions distracts from true worship.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses the tendency to ask why God created the universe. He firmly rejects the search for external causes behind God's actions, stating that such a search is impossible and diverts from our duty. Instead, he redirects us to what truly matters: knowing God, obeying Him, and preparing for the Day of Judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Maturidi first presents a question and a proposed answer before giving his own response.
The passage includes a quote from Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.
2
Identify the question and initial answer
The questioner asks about creation since God gains no benefit. The initial answer claims He created for eternal reward.
This answer is attributed to the Mu'tazilites.
3
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi says 'cause is impossible' because it would imply authority over God, and God's actions are inherently wise. Asking about wisdom is futile.
The word 'futile' is in brackets, indicating possible OCR uncertainty.
4
Recognize the positive duty
Instead of questioning, our duty is to know God, obey Him, and prepare for the final answer.
This includes 'right of lordship' and 'performance of obedience'.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi ends with 'There is no power but in God', emphasizing reliance on Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سئل عن خلقه الأشياء إذ لم يكن له فيه نفع ولا كان عابثًا به، فزعم أنه خلق للعرض على ثواب الأبد؛ وذلك حكمة، فيكون فعله لنفع يكون لخلقه، لا لعلة تقدمت الخلق وهو كإيجاد البنيان وأنواع الأشياء يحدث من العباد. {قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: } وقد بينا نحن ما يقتضي هذا الحرف من الجواب. على أن العلة محال؛ لإحالة أن يكون لأحد عليه سلطان، أو يخرج فعله عن الحكمة وبعد، فإن السؤال عن تعرف حكمة الربوبية [عبث]. وحق ربوبيته علينا معرفته ومعرفة حقه وأمره، والقيام بما علينا من طاعته وتعظيمه، والإعداد لحق الجواب في كل ما نقوله ونعمله. وذلك يشغلنا عن طلب الاعتلال له في فعله، أو الاحتجاج بالجواب عنه فيما تعدى السائل طوره، وأعرض عما عليه من أعذاره لفعله الذي هو مسؤول عنه مجزي به. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقوله: «خلق الخلق لنفع الخلق»، ونفعه ما ذكر، فإنه حيد عن الجواب، لأنه سئل عن خلق الأشياء، ومن ذكر فهم صنف من الجملة؛ فلذلك أوجب ذلك حيده.
Then he was asked about His creation of things, since He had no benefit in it nor was He playing with it, so he claimed that He created for the purpose of presenting it to eternal reward; and that is wisdom, so His action is for a benefit to His creation, not because of a cause that preceded creation, and it is like the construction of a building and types of things that occur from servants. {Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said:} And we have explained what this matter requires of an answer. However, cause is impossible, because it is impossible that anyone should have authority over Him, or that His action should depart from wisdom. Moreover, asking about knowing the wisdom of lordship is [futile]. And the right of His lordship upon us is to know Him, to know His right and command, and to perform what is upon us of obedience and exaltation, and to prepare for the right answer in everything we say and do. And that occupies us from seeking a cause for His action, or arguing by an answer about it when the questioner has exceeded his bounds and turned away from what is upon him of apologies for his action for which he will be asked and requited. And there is no power but in God. And his statement: 'He created creation for the benefit of creation'—and its benefit is what was mentioned—is a deviation from the answer, because he was asked about the creation of things, and mentioning them [that group] is a part of the whole; therefore that necessitated his deviation.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of why God created the universe. The questioner asks: since God does not benefit from creation and He is not playing, what is the purpose? The answer given by some (the Mu'tazilites?) is that He created for eternal reward, which is wisdom and a benefit for creation. However, al-Maturidi (through Abu Mansur) responds that this answer is insufficient. He argues that ascribing a cause (illa) to God's action is impossible because no one can have authority over God. Furthermore, seeking the wisdom behind God's actions is futile; our duty is to know God, obey Him, and prepare for the answer on the Day of Judgment. This preoccupies us from asking about reasons. The argument concludes that the answer 'He created for benefit' deviates from the original question, because the question was about the creation of all things, but the answer only mentions a specific group (those who receive reward). Thus it is a partial and incomplete answer.

Key Terms
علة (ʿilla)
حكمة (ḥikma)
ربوبية (rubūbiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can and should ask for the reasons behind God's actions.
Correction: Maturidi argues that seeking such reasons is impossible and diverts from worship.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, seeking a cause (illa) for God's action is:
A) permissible
B) impossible
C) obligatory
D) recommended

Key Takeaway
Seeking causes for God's actions is futile; our duty is worship and obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship or an unexplained event in your life, do you find yourself questioning why God allowed it? How does this passage's teaching that seeking causes for God's actions is futile challenge you to instead focus on obedience and exaltation? Will you commit to redirecting that energy toward knowing Him and preparing your answer?
?
Think of a time when you felt entitled to understand God's wisdom in a situation. Did that distract you from your actual duty? How can you, starting today, shift from asking 'why' to asking 'how should I respond in worship and obedience?'
?
The passage says that seeking causes occupies us from our true purpose. What specific questions about God's actions have been occupying your mind? Will you consciously set them aside and focus on what He has commanded you to do?

The Incomplete Justification: Benefit as a Partial Answer
Maturidi criticizes the answer that God created for benefit as a deviation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique that the claim 'God created for benefit' is a deviation because it addresses only part of creation.

Core Idea
A partial explanation cannot answer a universal question.

In the previous lesson, we saw Maturidi's general refutation of seeking causes. Here, he specifically criticizes the answer that God created for the benefit of creation. He argues that this answer is incomplete because the question was about the creation of all things, but the answer only applies to those who receive reward. Thus, it is a deviation (ḥayd) from the proper response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that this is a single sentence critiquing a specific claim.
2
Understand the original question
The original question was about the creation of 'things' (الأشياء), which is all-encompassing.
3
Identify the answer and its limitation
The answer 'He created for benefit' only mentions a specific group (those who receive reward), which is a subset of creation.
4
See why it is a deviation
Because the answer addresses only part of the whole, it deviates from the full scope of the question.
Maturidi calls this 'ḥayd' (deviation).

Proof from Variation in Rest and Motion
Maturidi answers an objection about rest and then refutes the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how variations in rest and motion demonstrate the need for a creator distinct from the first rest, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila claim of preservation without a preserver.

Core Idea
Change in rest and motion proves they are not eternal; a creator must exist.

In this passage, Maturidi responds to an objection about the nature of rest. He shows that if rest can vary in duration, then it is not that first rest itself. This leads to a refutation of the Mu'tazila who deny the need for a preserver. The argument uses simple observation of physical states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on rest and motion
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Focus on the logical structure.
Notice that Maturidi begins with an objection and then answers it.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument hinges on the fact that rest can increase or decrease in duration. This shows that the rest observed is not the original rest but something derived.
The author says 'ثبت أن ثمة غير السكون الأول'.
3
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila
Maturidi uses the otherness of rest and motion to argue that they are distinct and can substitute for each other. This invalidates the Mu'tazila claim that preservation can exist without a preserver.
The term 'بلا بقاء' refers to their denial of an attribute of preservation.
4
Reflect on the implication
If rest and motion are not eternal and depend on each other, then they point to a creator who brings them about.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب من قال: «لعل سكونه معه حيث كان بما ما قد يذكر مدة سكونه في مكان بزيادة ونقصان ثبت أن ثمة غير السكون الأول. وهذا مثل الأول لا يُسأل عنه، وجوابه ما بينا. والله المستعان.
ثم أطنب في هذا النوع، تركته لما لا منفعة فيه. وفيما قال من دليل غيرية السكون والحركة من جواز كون كل واحد منهما بدلا عن الآخر وأنهما غيران ما يُبطل قول كثير من المعتزلة في قولهم بالإبقاء بلا بقاء. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
ثم أجاب من عارضه بما كذلك كانت الأجسام غير خالية عما ذكرت أبدا. فزعم أنه لا يجوز، لما لا يثبت للكل شرط القدم إلا بوجود غير هو في ذلك، وفي ذلك بطلان الوجود. واستدل بما سبق ذكره من دخول الدار. مع ما زعم في طيرين يطيران بينهما ذراع من جهة واحدة طيرانا مستويا، لم يحتمل أن يكونا كذلك من غير نهاية لأوليتهما، إذ ارتفاع النهاية يوجب الاجتماع بالاستواء وقد وجد التفاضل.
واحتج بما إذ ثبت تضاد الأشياء من الثقل والخفة والحرارة والبرودة ونحو ذلك. وقد ثبت فساد كون الشيء من الشيء إلى ما لا أول له. ثم كان من طبع المتضاد التنافر، وفي ذلك التباعد وبخاصة إذ جعل أصحاب هذا القول اثنين متباينين فامتزجا وكانا متضادين، لم يجز لهما الاجتماع بما ذكر مع ما كان اختلافهما طباعا، ولو جاز خروجهما عن الطباع الذي ذكرت لجاز أن يسخن المبرد ويبرد المسخن، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز فناؤهما ليخرجا من طبع البقاء. وإذا بطل ذا ثبت قول أهل التوحيد في مدبر عليم ألف بين ذلك ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he answered the one who said: 'Perhaps its rest with it, wherever it was, by what may be mentioned of the duration of its rest in a place with increase and decrease, it is established that there is something other than the first rest. This is like the first, not to be asked about, and its answer is what we have clarified. And God is the Succour. Then he elaborated on this type, but I left it for no benefit in it. And in what he said of the proof of the otherness of rest and motion from the possibility of each one substituting for the other and that they are two others, that invalidates the saying of many of the Mu'tazila in their statement about preservation without a preserver. And there is no power except with God. Then he answered the one who objected by saying that likewise bodies are never devoid of what was mentioned. He claimed that it is not permissible, because the condition of eternity is not established for the whole except by the existence of something other than it in that, and in that is the nullification of existence. And he argued by what was previously mentioned of entering the house. Along with what he claimed about two birds flying between them a cubit from one direction in a uniform flight, it is not possible that they be like that without an end to their beginning, since the removal of the end necessitates meeting in uniformity, but difference has been found. And he argued by the fact that when the opposition of things is established – heaviness and lightness, heat and cold, and the like – and the corruption [of the possibility] of a thing from a thing to what has no beginning has been established, then it is from the nature of opposites to repel, and in that is separation, especially when the proponents of this saying made two distinct things that mixed and were opposites, it is not permissible for them to meet as mentioned, given that their difference was by nature. If it were permissible for them to depart from the nature mentioned, then it would be permissible for the cooler to heat and the heater to cool, and if that were permissible, then their annihilation would be permissible for them to depart from the nature of permanence. And if this is invalidated, then the saying of the people of tawhid is established: a wise manager who harmonized that. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a series of arguments against the claim that there are two eternal principles (likely light and darkness or similar dualism). The author begins by answering an objection about the nature of rest and motion, showing that variations in duration indicate something other than the original rest. He then refutes the Mu'tazila who deny an attribute of preservation. Next, he presents a proof using two birds flying in parallel; if they had no beginning, they would necessarily meet, but they differ, so there must be a beginning. Finally, he uses the opposition of physical properties (heavy/light, hot/cold) to show that two opposing natures cannot coexist eternally without contradiction. If they depart from their natures, the natural order would invert, leading to absurdities. Therefore, the correct view is that of tawhid: one wise governor who reconciled opposites.

Key Terms
سكون
حركة
إبقاء
بقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that God preserves the universe without an attribute of preservation (بقاء).
Correction: Maturidi argues that rest and motion are distinct and can substitute for each other, which shows that preservation requires a preserver.

Checkpoint
What does the variation in rest (increase or decrease) prove?
A) That rest is eternal.
B) That there is something other than the first rest.
C) That rest does not exist.
D) That rest and motion are the same.

Key Takeaway
Variation in physical states like rest points to a creator distinct from those states, refuting the idea that they are eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your rest is never constant; it varies in length and quality. Does this remind you that you are not self-sufficient, but dependent on the One whose rest is utterly transcendent? How will you consciously rely on Him today for your moments of stillness?
?
The Mu'tazila denied that God preserves with an enduring attribute, effectively implying creation sustains itself. In what areas of your life do you act as if your own efforts can preserve your faith, health, or relationships without God's constant attribute of preservation? What would change if you truly believed He is sustaining everything at every moment?
?
You experience both rest and motion every day. If these simple states cannot be eternal and require a Creator, what about your own existence? Are you living as if you are self-made, or as a creature utterly dependent on the Creator? What one habit can you change to acknowledge His absolute sovereignty over your activities?

Proof from Two Birds in Flight
Maturidi uses a thought experiment with two birds to prove a beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of two birds flying in parallel proves that the world must have a beginning.

Core Idea
If two things have no beginning, they would meet; but they differ, so there is a beginning.

Maturidi presents a clever argument using two birds flying at a constant distance. If they had no beginning, they would eventually coincide, but they maintain a difference, so they must have started at some point. This is a powerful refutation of the idea of an eternal universe.

Guided Path
1
Read the bird argument passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Visualize two birds flying side by side at a constant distance.
The key phrase: 'طيرين يطيران بينهما ذراع من جهة واحدة طيرانا مستويا'.
2
Understand the logical premise
The argument assumes uniform flight and constant distance. If they had no beginning, they would have been flying forever. Since they are at a constant distance, they would eventually occupy the same positions at infinity, meaning they would meet. But they differ, so they must have a beginning.
Think about infinite series: if two series are parallel, at infinity they coincide.
3
Identify the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that it is impossible for them to be like that without an end to their beginning. Thus, they have a beginning.
4
Connect to refuting dualism
If the world had no beginning, similar contradictions arise. This argument supports the existence of a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ثم أطنب في هذا النوع، تركته لما لا منفعة فيه. وفيما قال من دليل غيرية السكون والحركة من جواز كون كل واحد منهما بدلا عن الآخر وأنهما غيران ما يُبطل قول كثير من المعتزلة في قولهم بالإبقاء بلا بقاء. ولا قوة إلا بالله.

Then he answered the one who said: 'Perhaps its rest with it, wherever it was, by what may be mentioned of the duration of its rest in a place with increase and decrease, it is established that there is something other than the first rest. ?

?
The birds' constant separation is measured by a cubit—a fixed, finite unit. Are there fixed distances in your life (e.g., between you and obedience to Allah, or between you and repentance) that you maintain day after day, as if they could go on forever? When will you close that gap?
?
The argument proves that an infinite past is impossible—everything must have a beginning. Is there any sin, habit, or excuse in your life that you have labeled 'I've always been this way'? Will you continue to treat it as eternal, or will you finally seek repentance and start a new beginning today?

Proof from Opposition of Physical Properties
Maturidi argues from the nature of opposites to establish tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opposition of physical properties like heat and cold proves that two eternal principles cannot coexist, establishing the oneness of God.

Core Idea
Opposing natures cannot coexist eternally; a wise governor must reconcile them.

Maturidi uses the natural opposition of properties such as heaviness and lightness, heat and cold. If two eternal principles were opposite, they could not mix; but if they could change their natures, that would lead to absurd consequences like a cooler heating. Thus, there must be one wise manager who harmonizes opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on opposites
Read the Arabic passage about heaviness, lightness, heat, cold. Understand the concept of opposition.
Key terms: تضاد, ثقل, خفة, حرارة, برودة.
2
Identify the impossibility of infinite regress
Maturidi states that it is corrupt to have a thing coming from another infinitely. This is a premise.
وقد ثبت فساد كون الشيء من الشيء إلى ما لا أول له.
3
Understand the nature of opposites
By nature, opposites repel each other and seek separation. If two opposite principles are mixed, they cannot coexist unless their natures are changed.
من طبع المتضاد التنافر.
4
Consider the hypothetical change
If it were possible for opposites to change their natures, then the cooler could heat and the heater could cool, which is absurd. This would also lead to their annihilation.
يجوز أن يسخن المبرد ويبرد المسخن.
5
Conclude the proof
Since these absurdities are impossible, the only valid conclusion is that there is a wise manager who created and harmonized opposites. This is the doctrine of tawhid.
ثبت قول أهل التوحيد في مدبر عليم ألف بين ذلك.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see opposites every day—light and dark, joy and sorrow. Do you ever let the tension of these opposites make you doubt God's wisdom, or do you trust that He harmonizes them for a purpose you may not yet see? What would change if you truly believed that every contradiction in your life is held together by a wise Manager?
?
If you were to face a situation that feels utterly contradictory (like a good God allowing suffering), would you let that contradiction push you toward disbelief, or would you instead seek the hidden harmony? What does your reaction reveal about the condition of your tawhid?
?
When you reflect on your own nature, are you aware of the opposing tendencies within yourself (e.g., desire for comfort vs. drive for achievement)? How does recognizing that you yourself are a composition of opposites point you to the One who designed and balances you? Have you ever thanked Him for that balance?

Maturidi's Critique of Misapplying Divine Attributes to Hyle
Maturidi critiques the misapplication of divine attributes to hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that describing hyle with attributes similar to Allah leads to logical self-contradiction.

Core Idea
Hyle cannot be described like Allah without contradicting its own nature.

In this passage, Maturidi exposes a fundamental error in the Aristotelian hyle theory: its proponents borrowed descriptions from the people of tawhid for Allah and applied them to hyle. But by doing so, they created an unsustainable system. Let's see how Maturidi unravels their inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to understand Maturidi's argument. Note his rhetorical style and key terms.
2
Identify the misapplication
Find where Maturidi states that they used the descriptions of Allah for hyle. Notice how this leads to a contradiction.
3
Understand the core contradiction
Maturidi argues that they made what is beyond accidents into a substance, thereby losing its primordial state. Reflect on why this is problematic.
This is the heart of his critique.
4
Reflect on the outcome
Consider how this contradiction destroys the hyle theory from within. The theory cannot maintain its own premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : } فمن تأمل ما صار هؤلاء إليه علم أنهم أوتوا ذلك لجهلهم نعم الله فعموا عن سبيل الرشد ،فضلوا، ثم بعثتهم حيرة الضلال إلى الاستيناس بمثل هذا الخيال الذي لا يصير عليه عقل ولا يستجلبه هوى والله المستعان . ولولا ذلك ما الذي كان يُعرفهم أن ابتداء العالم ما ذكر ؟ ثم اسمه " الذي وصف ثمة فيه ما ذكر، وعمله الذي نُعت ليس في جوهر العالم دليله ولا في السمع احتماله. لكنهم سمعوا قول أهل التوحيد في وصف الله بالذي وصفوا به الهيولى عندهم ، ولم ينظروا فيما ألزمهم القول به، فرجعوا فنقضوا ما قد أثبتوه؛ إذ صيروا الذي لذاته خارج عن احتمال الأعراض ممتنع عن معنى الجواهر جوهرا ثم جوهرا ثم جواهر ، ثم صار بحيث لم يبق من أوليته أثر . وما بقى مما انتهى [إليه] أمر العالم من القديم والحديث إلا الجواهر والأعراض، وذهب الذي لم يكن بهذا الوصف. فيكون في ذلك فناء العالم بنفسه ، واستحالة القديم بذاته بأعراض قهرته وأفنته مما لا قيام لها بنفسها. ويكون في ذلك القولُ بحدث جميع العالم، الذي دفعهم عظم هذا القول إلى ذلك الخيال؛ إذ كل ما هو مأخوذ إنما هو عرض وجوهر، ولم يكن الأول .. ثم يبطل قوله إذا نفسه حكيما ألزم غيره الصدود عن رأيه والاتباع هواه، بعد قوله : إن الأصل الذي منه كان كان جاهلاً سفيها ، وإن الأعراض هي أغيار ولّدتها القوة
The jurist (may Allah have mercy on him) said: So whoever considers what these people have come to knows that they came to that due to their ignorance of Allah's blessings, so they were blind to the path of guidance, and went astray, then the confusion of misguidance drove them to seek comfort in such an imagination that no intellect can settle upon nor can any desire attract it. And Allah is the seeker of help. Had it not been for that, what would have made them know that the beginning of the world was as mentioned? Then its name which was described there contains what was mentioned, and its action which was described is not in the substance of the world its proof, nor in hearing its possibility. Rather, they heard the statement of the people of tawhid in describing Allah as they described the hyle in their view, and they did not consider what their statement obliged them to, so they returned and destroyed what they had established; for they made that which by its essence is beyond the possibility of accidents, immune to the meaning of substances, into a substance, then a substance, then substances, then it became such that no trace of its primordality remained. And nothing remained of what the world's affair ended to, of the eternal and the temporal, except substances and accidents, and that which was not with this description departed. And in that is the annihilation of the world by itself, and the alteration of the eternal by itself through accidents that overcame it and annihilated it, which have no subsistence in themselves. And in that is the statement of the temporality of the entire world, which the magnitude of this statement drove them to that imagination; since everything that is perceived is only accident and substance, and the First was not. Then his statement is invalidated, since he himself makes himself wise, obligating others to turn away from his opinion and follow his desire, after his statement: that the origin from which it came was ignorant and foolish, and that accidents are changes produced by the power.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is Maturidi's theological refutation of the Aristotelian hyle theory. He begins by attributing their error to ignorance of Allah's blessings. He then points out a key contradiction: they describe hyle with attributes similar to those used by the people of tawhid for Allah, yet they fail to see that if hyle is eternal and formless, its transformation into substances with accidents means it loses its original state—amounting to self-annihilation. Thus, the world would be annihilated by itself, and the eternal would change by accidents, which is absurd. Moreover, they claim the origin (hyle) is ignorant, yet they themselves claim wisdom. Finally, Maturidi notes that their system implies the temporality of the entire world, which they wanted to avoid, so their argument is self-defeating.

Key Terms
هيولى
جوهر
عرض
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that philosophical speculation about the world's origin is neutral and can be reconciled with Islamic theology.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the hyle theory, when examined, leads to contradictions that undermine tawhid.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what did the proponents of hyle theory mistakenly do?
A) They denied the existence of God
B) They applied descriptions of Allah to hyle
C) They proved the world is eternal
D) They rejected all accidents

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that describing hyle with attributes of Allah leads to an internal contradiction that destroys the theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi points out that the philosophers' error began with ignorance of Allah's blessings. When was the last time you truly reflected on a blessing from Allah as a starting point for understanding His greatness? What specific blessing will you contemplate today, and how will it change your worship?
?
Do you ever find yourself describing God with attributes that actually diminish His transcendence, perhaps by projecting human qualities onto Him without realizing it? Search your heart: have you subtly felt that Allah thinks or acts like a human? What step will you take this week to purify your understanding of His names?
?
The philosophers held contradictory beliefs about hyle without noticing the inconsistency. Are there areas in your faith where you hold conflicting ideas—for example, trusting in Allah's provision while worrying excessively about your sustenance? Examine one such inconsistency in your life and commit to resolving it by seeking knowledge or through sincere supplication.

The Self-Annihilation of the World in the Hyle Theory
Maturidi exposes the logical absurdities of the hyle theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the hyle theory implies the world's annihilation and the alteration of the eternal, which is absurd.

Core Idea
If hyle changes into substances, the eternal becomes temporal, contradicting itself.

Maturidi continues his refutation by drawing out the consequences of the hyle theory. If the eternal hyle transforms into substances and accidents, then the world would be annihilated by itself, and the eternal would change—both impossibilities. Moreover, the theory's proponents claim wisdom but posit an ignorant origin. Let's examine these contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to follow Maturidi's logical consequences.
2
Identify the consequences
Find where Maturidi says the world is annihilated and the eternal is altered. Notice the language of self-annihilation.
3
Understand the absurdity
Reflect on why an eternal thing changing is impossible. Also, how an ignorant origin leads to a contradiction with their claim of wisdom.
4
See the self-defeating nature
Maturidi concludes that their argument is invalid because it forces them to accept the world's temporality, which they sought to avoid.
This shows the hyle theory is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : } فمن تأمل ما صار هؤلاء إليه علم أنهم أوتوا ذلك لجهلهم نعم الله فعموا عن سبيل الرشد ،فضلوا، ثم بعثتهم حيرة الضلال إلى الاستيناس بمثل هذا الخيال الذي لا يصير عليه عقل ولا يستجلبه هوى والله المستعان . ولولا ذلك ما الذي كان يُعرفهم أن ابتداء العالم ما ذكر ؟ ثم اسمه " الذي وصف ثمة فيه ما ذكر، وعمله الذي نُعت ليس في جوهر العالم دليله ولا في السمع احتماله. لكنهم سمعوا قول أهل التوحيد في وصف الله بالذي وصفوا به الهيولى عندهم ، ولم ينظروا فيما ألزمهم القول به، فرجعوا فنقضوا ما قد أثبتوه؛ إذ صيروا الذي لذاته خارج عن احتمال الأعراض ممتنع عن معنى الجواهر جوهرا ثم جوهرا ثم جواهر ، ثم صار بحيث لم يبق من أوليته أثر . وما بقى مما انتهى [إليه] أمر العالم من القديم والحديث إلا الجواهر والأعراض، وذهب الذي لم يكن بهذا الوصف. فيكون في ذلك فناء العالم بنفسه ، واستحالة القديم بذاته بأعراض قهرته وأفنته مما لا قيام لها بنفسها. ويكون في ذلك القولُ بحدث جميع العالم، الذي دفعهم عظم هذا القول إلى ذلك الخيال؛ إذ كل ما هو مأخوذ إنما هو عرض وجوهر، ولم يكن الأول .. ثم يبطل قوله إذا نفسه حكيما ألزم غيره الصدود عن رأيه والاتباع هواه، بعد قوله : إن الأصل الذي منه كان كان جاهلاً سفيها ، وإن الأعراض هي أغيار ولّدتها القوة
The jurist (may Allah have mercy on him) said: So whoever considers what these people have come to knows that they came to that due to their ignorance of Allah's blessings, so they were blind to the path of guidance, and went astray, then the confusion of misguidance drove them to seek comfort in such an imagination that no intellect can settle upon nor can any desire attract it. And Allah is the seeker of help. Had it not been for that, what would have made them know that the beginning of the world was as mentioned? Then its name which was described there contains what was mentioned, and its action which was described is not in the substance of the world its proof, nor in hearing its possibility. Rather, they heard the statement of the people of tawhid in describing Allah as they described the hyle in their view, and they did not consider what their statement obliged them to, so they returned and destroyed what they had established; for they made that which by its essence is beyond the possibility of accidents, immune to the meaning of substances, into a substance, then a substance, then substances, then it became such that no trace of its primordality remained. And nothing remained of what the world's affair ended to, of the eternal and the temporal, except substances and accidents, and that which was not with this description departed. And in that is the annihilation of the world by itself, and the alteration of the eternal by itself through accidents that overcame it and annihilated it, which have no subsistence in themselves. And in that is the statement of the temporality of the entire world, which the magnitude of this statement drove them to that imagination; since everything that is perceived is only accident and substance, and the First was not. Then his statement is invalidated, since he himself makes himself wise, obligating others to turn away from his opinion and follow his desire, after his statement: that the origin from which it came was ignorant and foolish, and that accidents are changes produced by the power.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is Maturidi's theological refutation of the Aristotelian hyle theory. He begins by attributing their error to ignorance of Allah's blessings. He then points out a key contradiction: they describe hyle with attributes similar to those used by the people of tawhid for Allah, yet they fail to see that if hyle is eternal and formless, its transformation into substances with accidents means it loses its original state—amounting to self-annihilation. Thus, the world would be annihilated by itself, and the eternal would change by accidents, which is absurd. Moreover, they claim the origin (hyle) is ignorant, yet they themselves claim wisdom. Finally, Maturidi notes that their system implies the temporality of the entire world, which they wanted to avoid, so their argument is self-defeating.

فناء العالم
قديم
جوهر
عرض

Misconception: The hyle theory seems to explain how the world came to be without needing a creator.
Correction: Maturidi shows it leads to logical absurdities and ultimately implies the world's temporality, which they wanted to avoid.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim is a consequence of the hyle theory?
A) The world becomes eternal
B) The world is annihilated by itself
C) The origin becomes wise
D) Substances disappear

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the hyle theory leads to self-annihilation and contradiction, making it untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief, have you ever followed it to its logical end only to discover it contradicts itself? Is there a belief you currently cling to that, if fully examined, would fall apart? Will you have the courage to abandon it today?
?
Maturidi points out the absurdity of calling the origin 'ignorant and foolish' while claiming wisdom for oneself. In what area of your life do you claim understanding but live as though the source of truth is foolish? What would it cost you to align your life with the truth you already know?
?
The hyle theory sought to avoid acknowledging that the entire world is temporal and dependent on the Creator. Are there parts of your life you try to keep independent from Allah, fearing that full submission would mean annihilation? What if that annihilation is actually the only way to true existence with Him?

The Contradiction of the Lower Principle
Maturidi refutes dualism via the lower principle's contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes dualism by showing that the lower principle (darkness) cannot both retain its nature and act against it.

Core Idea
If the lower becomes like the higher, dualism collapses.

Maturidi begins by examining the fundamental nature of the two supposed principles. The dualists claim darkness descends and light ascends. But in their own story, darkness acts against its nature, thus losing its identity and destroying the basis for a separate principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the focus on the natures of the lower and higher.
The passage uses 'suf'la' for lower and 'ulu' for higher.
2
Identify the contradiction
Find where Maturidi points out that the lower goes upward. This contradicts its nature of descending. Ask: what does this imply about the distinction between the two principles?
The contradiction is internal to the dualist account.
3
Understand the invalidation
Maturidi concludes that the lower has acquired both natures, so the basis for dualism (two distinct natures) is destroyed. Reflect on why this is a logical refutation rather than an empirical one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن كان من طبع السفلى التسفّل والعلوى العلو - وذلك معنى التنافر وإليه مرجع العاقبة - فكيف صار السفلى يذهب صعدًا، وذلك طبع العالى الصافي، وهو معنى الخير؛ فقد صار من السفلى الأمران جميعا، فبطل المعنى الذي له لزم القول باثنين. ثم من [طبع] العلوى النّفار إلى العلوّ، ولم يقم بوفاء ذلك ولا امتنع به عما كان بجوهر ينحدر حتى ارتفع عليه، وخلق العالم بحبسه؛ فكيف يطمعون أن يتخلص من يدى الهمامة؟ وهي مع ذلك حساسة فعالة بالحيل أوثقته وقيدته وحبسته وليست له قوة يتخلص بها، وبطبعه لم يمتنع عند التخلية؛ فكيف يتخلص بعد الوثاق؟ إلا أن يقول: تُخلّى الهمامة سبيله، فيجعلها فاعلة الخير.
Then if it is of the nature of the lower to descend and of the higher to ascend—and that is the meaning of repulsion and to it returns the ultimate outcome—then how has the lower come to go upward, while that is the nature of the pure higher, and it is the meaning of good? Then the lower has acquired both matters together, so the meaning by which dualism was necessitated is invalidated. Then, of the [nature] of the higher is aversion to the heights, and it did not fulfill that nor did it refrain by it from what was, by essence, descending until it rose above it, and it created the world by imprisoning it; so how can they hope to escape from the hands of the owl? Yet it, along with that, is sensitive, effective through devices; it bound him, shackled him, imprisoned him, and he has no power by which to escape, and by his nature he did not resist at the time of release; so how can he escape after being bound? Unless he say: the owl releases his way, thus making it a doer of good.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents two interconnected arguments against the dualist claim that the world is composed of two eternal principles, light and darkness. The author first addresses the natural tendencies of these principles: the lower (darkness) is descend by nature, the higher (light) ascends. This mutual repulsion is their defining characteristic. However, in the dualist account, the lower somehow goes upward, which contradicts its nature. If it does, it acquires the nature of the higher, thus losing its distinct identity and invalidating the basis for claiming two separate principles. The author then points out that the light, despite its natural upward tendency, is described as being imprisoned by darkness. Its nature should cause it to ascend and resist imprisonment, but it does not. The owl (a symbolic name for the force of darkness) is portrayed as cunning and effective, binding the light without resistance. The light cannot escape even when released, let alone when bound. This shows that the light's nature is not actually as claimed, or the dualist story is incoherent. The argument proceeds in the order of the dualist premises, highlighting their contradictions.

Key Terms
طبائع
السفلى
العلوى
التنافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument relies on a physical analogy rather than logical contradiction.
Correction: The argument shows internal inconsistency in the dualist's own assumptions, not a physical law.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the fact that the lower principle goes upward imply about the dualist claim?
A) The lower principle is actually good
B) The lower principle has lost its defining nature, thus the basis for dualism is destroyed
C) The lower principle is stronger than the higher
D) The higher principle is actually evil

Key Takeaway
The dualist's own narrative undermines their position by showing the lower principle acts against its nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life do you maintain a false distinction—between 'my nature' and 'what I actually do'—that collapses under honest examination? Will you let that contradiction stand, or will you bring your actions into alignment with what you claim to believe?
?
If the lower principle can act against its 'nature' to seek good, what excuse do you have for remaining enslaved to your lower self? What concrete step will you take today to rise above a habit you once thought was unchangeable?
?
The dualist's entire worldview crumbled because of one inconsistency. Is there an inconsistency in your own faith and practice that, if left unchanged, would invalidate your profession of tawhid? What will you do about it before you meet Allah?

The Contradiction of the Higher Principle
Maturidi refutes dualism via the higher principle's weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi further refutes dualism by showing that the higher principle (light) is described as weak and imprisoned, contradicting its supposed nature.

Core Idea
The light's inability to resist darkness disproves its claimed nature.

Maturidi turns to the higher principle. The dualists claim light naturally ascends and is pure good. Yet they describe it as being imprisoned by darkness, unable to escape. This inconsistency reveals the falsity of their entire framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the description of the higher's natural aversion and its failure to act accordingly.
'Nafār' means aversion or repulsion.
2
Analyze the imprisonment
Identify how the higher is described as being bound and unable to escape. Contrast this with its claimed nature of ascending.
The 'owl' (hammah) is a symbolic name for darkness.
3
Understand the final point
Maturidi notes that if the dualist claims the darkness releases the light, that would make darkness good, contradicting its evil nature. This closes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents two interconnected arguments against the dualist claim that the world is composed of two eternal principles, light and darkness. The author first addresses the natural tendencies of these principles: the lower (darkness) is descend by nature, the higher (light) ascends. This mutual repulsion is their defining characteristic. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Maturidi shows that if darkness can do good (release the light), then evil is not a fixed nature. Have you ever blamed a person or situation as 'pure evil' instead of seeing that only Allah determines good and evil? Repent of this now and ask Allah to align your heart with His decree.
?
The 'higher principle' in the dualist narrative is weak and imprisoned. The Dilemma of Knowledge and Power
Maturidi presents a logical dilemma on God's knowledge and power regarding Iblis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that Maturidi poses to dualists: if God knew Iblis would cause evil and allowed it, that implies evil; if He didn't know, ignorance is evil; if He could prevent but didn't, that is evil; if He couldn't, He is incapable and not Lord.

Core Idea
God's permission of Iblis cannot be justified without implying evil or weakness in God.

Maturidi begins his refutation by presenting a series of logical possibilities regarding God's knowledge and power over Iblis. Each possibility leads to a conclusion that either attributes evil to God or denies His lordship. This establishes the fundamental problem with the dualist claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Maturidi's dense reasoning. Note the logical alternatives he presents.
This passage contains several alternative scenarios.
2
Identify the four possibilities
Find the two main branches: knowledge (know vs not know) and power (able vs unable). Then note the sub-cases under knowledge.
3
Analyze the implications
For each possibility, understand why Maturidi concludes it leads to either evil or incapability. Ask yourself: what does this say about the dualist claim?
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this dilemma undermines the idea that God can permit Iblis without fault. How does it force the dualist into a contradiction?
This connects to the lesson's core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو علم فتركه على ما علم من الفساد به، فذلك منه شر. ومثله إما أن يكون علم من قبل ما يعمل فكره، ففكر على العلم بما يكون منه، وهو شر، وإما لم يعلم، والجهل شر. ثم لا يخلو من أن يكون قادرا على منع إبليس وقهره أو لا. فإن قدر ثم أمهله ليفسد الخلق فهو شر عندهم، وإن لم يقدر فلا يكون العاجز رب العالمين. مع ما يقال: ثم علم أن إبليس عند المدة يفى له بالذي وعد، ووفاء الوعد خير وحق، فإذا يكون من الشر ذلك. مع ما كان هذا لازما له، لأنه إذا كان ممن هو أصل الخير يجئ الشر، فنعكس عليهم ونجعل كل خير من إبليس، وكل شر من غيره. وبعد، فكيف يأمن بالقدرة عليه في الوقت الذي لم يكن لإبليس غير نفسه عون وللذي به كان كل الأشياء أعوان؟ ثم اختلط خلقه الذين هم أعوانه بالذين هم أعوان الله في منعهم عن المعونة عليه. جل الله عما وصفه الملحدون. وإن قالوا: الموادعة كانت لبعض المصالح، فمثله الهوام الضارة والأشياء المؤذية. وبعد، فإن تخوفه من يضاده يوجب الجهل بأنه رب كل شيء؛ وكذلك إصابة العين، فإذا ضر به العين ومن تقهره العين، وتزيل قدرته، وتدفع علمه فهو رب بغيره لا بنفسه، خالق بغيره فيلزم القول في معبودهم: إنه عبد لا معبود.
Or if He knew and left him upon what He knew of corruption, then that from Him is evil. And likewise, either He knew beforehand what he would do, so He thought about the knowledge of what would be from him, and that is evil, or He did not know, and ignorance is evil. Then it is unavoidable that either He is powerful to prevent Iblis and subdue him or not. If He is powerful then He gave him respite to corrupt creation, then that is evil according to them, and if He is not powerful, then the incapable one cannot be the Lord of the worlds. Along with what is said: Then He knew that Iblis during the term would fulfill what he was promised, and fulfilling the promise is good and right, thus evil comes from that. Likewise, this is necessary for Him, because if He is the one from whom good originates, evil comes, then we reverse it upon them and make every good from Iblis, and every evil from other than him. And moreover, how can He be confident in power over him at a time when Iblis had no helper other than himself, while He by whom all things exist has helpers? Then His creatures who are His helpers mixed with those who are God's helpers in preventing them from assisting Him. Exalted is God above what the atheists describe. And if they say: The conciliation was for some benefits, then similarly are harmful vermin and injurious things. And moreover, His fear of one who opposes Him necessitates ignorance that He is Lord of everything; and likewise the affliction of the eye, so if the eye harms Him and those whom the eye overpowers, and it removes His power and repels His knowledge, then He is a lord by other than Himself, not by Himself, a creator by other than Himself, so it follows that their object of worship is a servant, not a worshipped one.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Magian dualist claim that God's permission of evil (specifically Iblis) indicates either evil or weakness in God. Maturidi presents a series of objections and responses. First, he argues that if God knew Iblis would cause evil and still allowed him, that would be evil; if He did not know, ignorance is evil; if He could prevent but did not, that is evil; if He could not prevent, He is imperfect. Then he addresses a counterargument: that God's promise to Iblis of respite necessitated fulfillment, which is good. But if that is so, then good comes from God yet evil also comes, contradicting the dualist claim that good and evil have separate sources. Maturidi then reverses the argument: if Iblis's good action (fulfilling a promise) comes from God, then all good could be from Iblis. Next, he questions how God could be powerful over Iblis if He needed helpers, whereas Iblis had none. The mixture of helpers implies dependence, which is incompatible with divinity. Finally, he argues that if evil beings can harm God or overpower Him, then God would be dependent on others, which reduces Him to a servant. Thus the dualists' alleged deity of evil is actually a dependent being.

Key Terms
شر
إبليس
قدرة
عجز
رب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's allowance of Iblis to act shows God is evil or weak.
Correction: Maturidi shows that every possible scenario leads to a contradiction, thereby proving that the dualist's framework is flawed.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's refutation, if God were unable to prevent Iblis from corrupting creation, what would follow?
A) God would be evil
B) God would be incapable and not Lord
C) Iblis would be God
D) Evil would not exist

Key Takeaway
The dilemma shows that God's permission of Iblis cannot be rationalized without attributing evil or incapability to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly blamed God for a painful event in your life, thinking He could have prevented it but didn't? After reading this lesson, how will you respond the next time such thoughts arise?
?
The argument shows that accusing God of evil leads to absurd conclusions. Is there any area of your life where you still hold God responsible for something you consider unfair? Write down a specific hardship and commit to trusting His wisdom despite not understanding it.
?
If God's permission of Iblis is not evil, then what does that say about your own tendency to question God's decisions? Will you now stop measuring God's actions by your limited sense of justice and instead submit fully to His sovereignty? Make a concrete resolve today.

The Promise and the Reversal of the Argument
Maturidi refutes the justification of God's permission via promise and reverses the dualist attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi counters the argument that God's promise to Iblis justifies His permission, and how he reverses the dualist claim to show its absurdity.

Core Idea
If fulfilling a promise to Iblis justifies good, then the same logic makes Iblis the source of good.

The dualists might argue that God's permission of Iblis was for the sake of fulfilling a promise, which is good. Maturidi turns this argument on its head: if that is good, then evil also comes from God, and one could equally claim that all good comes from Iblis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and the translation
Focus on the part where Maturidi mentions 'what is said' – a possible objection from dualists.
2
Understand the dualist objection
The dualists claim that God's permission is actually good because it fulfills a promise. Identify the premise and conclusion.
3
Grasp Maturidi's reversal
Maturidi points out that if this argument is accepted, then evil also comes from God. Moreover, one could reverse the attribution: Iblis becomes the source of good.
This is a powerful rhetorical technique.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this reversal undermines the dualist framework. How does it show that the dualist attribution of good and evil to separate sources is arbitrary?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو علم فتركه على ما علم من الفساد به، فذلك منه شر. ومثله إما أن يكون علم من قبل ما يعمل فكره، ففكر على العلم بما يكون منه، وهو شر، وإما لم يعلم، والجهل شر. ثم لا يخلو من أن يكون قادرا على منع إبليس وقهره أو لا. فإن قدر ثم أمهله ليفسد الخلق فهو شر عندهم، وإن لم يقدر فلا يكون العاجز رب العالمين. مع ما يقال: ثم علم أن إبليس عند المدة يفى له بالذي وعد، ووفاء الوعد خير وحق، فإذا يكون من الشر ذلك. مع ما كان هذا لازما له، لأنه إذا كان ممن هو أصل الخير يجئ الشر، فنعكس عليهم ونجعل كل خير من إبليس، وكل شر من غيره. وبعد، فكيف يأمن بالقدرة عليه في الوقت الذي لم يكن لإبليس غير نفسه عون وللذي به كان كل الأشياء أعوان؟ ثم اختلط خلقه الذين هم أعوانه بالذين هم أعوان الله في منعهم عن المعونة عليه. جل الله عما وصفه الملحدون. وإن قالوا: الموادعة كانت لبعض المصالح، فمثله الهوام الضارة والأشياء المؤذية. وبعد، فإن تخوفه من يضاده يوجب الجهل بأنه رب كل شيء؛ وكذلك إصابة العين، فإذا ضر به العين ومن تقهره العين، وتزيل قدرته، وتدفع علمه فهو رب بغيره لا بنفسه، خالق بغيره فيلزم القول في معبودهم: إنه عبد لا معبود.
Or if He knew and left him upon what He knew of corruption, then that from Him is evil. And likewise, either He knew beforehand what he would do, so He thought about the knowledge of what would be from him, and that is evil, or He did not know, and ignorance is evil. Then it is unavoidable that either He is powerful to prevent Iblis and subdue him or not. If He is powerful then He gave him respite to corrupt creation, then that is evil according to them, and if He is not powerful, then the incapable one cannot be the Lord of the worlds. Along with what is said: Then He knew that Iblis during the term would fulfill what he was promised, and fulfilling the promise is good and right, thus evil comes from that. Likewise, this is necessary for Him, because if He is the one from whom good originates, evil comes, then we reverse it upon them and make every good from Iblis, and every evil from other than him. And moreover, how can He be confident in power over him at a time when Iblis had no helper other than himself, while He by whom all things exist has helpers? Then His creatures who are His helpers mixed with those who are God's helpers in preventing them from assisting Him. Exalted is God above what the atheists describe. And if they say: The conciliation was for some benefits, then similarly are harmful vermin and injurious things. And moreover, His fear of one who opposes Him necessitates ignorance that He is Lord of everything; and likewise the affliction of the eye, so if the eye harms Him and those whom the eye overpowers, and it removes His power and repels His knowledge, then He is a lord by other than Himself, not by Himself, a creator by other than Himself, so it follows that their object of worship is a servant, not a worshipped one.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Magian dualist claim that God's permission of evil (specifically Iblis) indicates either evil or weakness in God. Maturidi presents a series of objections and responses. First, he argues that if God knew Iblis would cause evil and still allowed him, that would be evil; if He did not know, ignorance is evil; if He could prevent but did not, that is evil; if He could not prevent, He is imperfect. Then he addresses a counterargument: that God's promise to Iblis of respite necessitated fulfillment, which is good. But if that is so, then good comes from God yet evil also comes, contradicting the dualist claim that good and evil have separate sources. Maturidi then reverses the argument: if Iblis's good action (fulfilling a promise) comes from God, then all good could be from Iblis. Next, he questions how God could be powerful over Iblis if He needed helpers, whereas Iblis had none. The mixture of helpers implies dependence, which is incompatible with divinity. Finally, he argues that if evil beings can harm God or overpower Him, then God would be dependent on others, which reduces Him to a servant. Thus the dualists' alleged deity of evil is actually a dependent being.

Key Terms
شر
خير
إبليس
وعد
رب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's permission of Iblis is justified by the goodness of fulfilling a promise.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this justification leads to the absurd conclusion that Iblis could be the source of all good.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi do in response to the dualist argument that God's permission is good because it fulfills a promise?
A) He accepts the argument
B) He reverses the attribution of good and evil
C) He denies that God made a promise
D) He says the promise was evil

Key Takeaway
The dualists' own logic can be turned against them, showing that their attribution of good and evil to separate sources is inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself blaming something external (a person, circumstance, or 'shaytan') for an evil you've done, how does Maturidi's reversal expose the inconsistency in your own heart? What would it mean for you to stop attributing evil to others and accept your own role?
?
If you were to reverse the logic in your own life and trace every good back to a being you consider evil (like Iblis), how would that feel? What does that say about your need for a consistent understanding of good and evil?
?
The dualists tried to honor God by distancing Him from evil, but ended up undermining His sovereignty. Are there ways you subtly distance God from certain parts of your life—by attributing your successes to yourself and your failures to circumstances? How does this passage call you to a more absolute reliance on God?

The Dependence of the Alleged Evil Deity
Maturidi argues that the dualists' object of worship is dependent and therefore not divine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the dualists' object of worship (the source of evil) is actually a dependent being, not a true god.

Core Idea
If the source of evil is powerful only with helpers, and can be harmed, then it is a servant, not a lord.

Maturidi goes on to argue that the dualists' alleged deity of evil is not self-sufficient. He points out that Iblis had no helpers, while God has many; yet the dualists' object of worship is depicted as needing assistance, being harmed, and losing power. This reduces it to a created being, not a true lord.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Pay attention to the contrasts: Iblis has no helpers, God has helpers. Also note the examples of harmful creatures and the eye.
2
Analyze the contrast in helpers
Consider why having helpers implies dependence. How does this undermine divinity?
3
Understand the argument about fear and harm
If the dualist deity can fear an opponent or be harmed (by the eye), then it is not self-sufficient.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
Maturidi concludes that such a being is a lord by others, a creator by others, hence a servant, not a true object of worship.
This seals the refutation.

The Necessity of a Wise Creator for Harmful Things
Maturidi argues for a wise creator of harmful things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi insists that harmful things must be attributed to a wise creator rather than an independent originator of evil.

Core Idea
Harmful things are not independent evils; they are part of a wise plan that balances harm and benefit.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise argument that harmful things cannot exist without a wise creator. He challenges the idea that evil has an independent source, instead showing that all creation, including harmful things, is under the management of a knowing and wise Creator. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing deeply. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's direct claim.
2
Identify key terms
Notice the terms 'مدبر' (wise manager) and 'منشئ الشر' (originator of evil). Contrast them to see the key opposition in the argument.
3
Analyze the logic
Maturidi argues that harmful things are placed by a wise manager who makes them cause harm to some and benefit others. Therefore, the claim of an independent originator of evil is false. Ask yourself how this refutes the idea of independent evil.
This connects to the learning objective.

Harmful (things), but by a wise, knowing manager who made them so that they harm one and benefit another. It is established that the statement that the originator of evil is alone (in creating them) is far (from truth).
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a brief argument establishing the necessity of a wise creator for harmful things. Maturidi argues that harmful things are not self-originating but are placed by a wise, knowing manager (mudabbir) who makes them harm some and benefit others. Therefore, the claim that evil has a sole originator (munshi' al-sharr) who creates it independently is far-fetched and incorrect. The conclusion is that evil is part of a broader wise plan.

Misconception: Some believe that evil or harmful things are created independently by a separate power (e.g., Satan or an independent originator), separate from the one true God.
Correction: Maturidi argues that harmful things are not created by an independent source; rather, they are created by a wise, knowing manager (Allah) who designs them to cause harm in some contexts and benefit in others, refuting the notion of an independent originator of evil.

D) Because harmful things are illusions.

Key Takeaway
Harmful things are not evidence of an independent evil force; they are part of a wise, created order that balances harm and benefit.

The Wisdom in Harmful Creation: Refuting Dualists and Mu'tazilites
Maturidi refutes dualists and Mu'tazilites who deny wisdom in harmful creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the dualist and Mu'tazilite objections to divine wisdom in creation, arguing that all harm contains hidden benefit.

Core Idea
No harm exists without hidden wisdom; human judgment is too limited to label any act as foolish.

Imam Maturidi addresses a fundamental challenge: how can a wise God create harmful things? Both dualists and some Mu'tazilites have argued that harmful actions cannot be called wise. In this passage, Maturidi dismantles their reasoning by showing that human perception cannot fully grasp the wisdom behind creation, and that every harm holds a potential benefit, whether as a lesson, reminder, or sign.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the original Arabic carefully, then the literal translation, to grasp Maturidi's dense argumentative style.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'ف' and 'وإذ'.
2
Identify the two main refutations
Notice that Maturidi first refutes the dualists who believe in two eternal principles, then the Mu'tazilites who claim only beneficial actions are wise. Look for key terms like 'الثنوية' and 'المعتزلة'.
3
Understand the epistemological premise
Maturidi begins by establishing that humans cannot definitively label an act as wise or foolish, just or unjust, because their knowledge is limited. This is the foundation for both refutations.
This is a key principle in Maturidi theology: divine transcendence beyond human categories.
4
Reflect on the hidden benefits of harm
Maturidi lists several ways harm can benefit: through indication, admonition, reminding of blessings, or warning of punishment. Consider how these apply to everyday experiences.
Think of examples where a difficulty taught you something valuable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجه ذلك على الناظر المتأمل، أو هو بالحس يريد الاطلاع على العلم به. وقد ثبت احتمال الوجهين، لا يقع على واحد منهما الحس وعلم المتأمل ذلك، [ف]بطل قضاؤه في شريعته على الإشارة إليه بالحكمة والسفه والعدل والجور فلزم بهذا جهل كل من البشر المعرفة حقيقة الأمرين في الشيء بالتأمل فيه [مثل] أن يعرف جميع الأسباب التي بها تتغير أحوال المحسوسات على الحواس. وإذا ثبت ذا بطل قول الثنوية بالاثنين [ وثبت] جهلهم بوجوه الحكمة في خلق الضار والنافع، إذ قد يجوز أن يصير كل ضار في حال نافعا في أخرى. وبطل [قول] من يقول من المعتزلة : إن كل فعل لا ينفع آخر فهو غير حكمة. مع ما لا يوجد ضرر ألبتة إلا وأمكن أن ينتفع به أحد، إما من طريق الدلالة، أو من طريق الموعظة، أوما فيه من تذكير النعمة وتحذير النقمة، ومن تعريف من له الخلق والأمر في الخلق، وغير ذلك مما يكثر ذكره. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The examination of that is incumbent upon the reflective observer, or he intends by perception to attain knowledge of it. And the possibility of the two aspects has been established; perception and the knowledge of the reflective observer do not fall upon one of them. So his judgment in his law regarding the indication of it by wisdom and foolishness and justice and injustice is invalidated. Thus it necessarily follows that every human is ignorant of the knowledge of the true nature of the two matters in a thing through reflection upon it, [such as] knowing all the causes by which the states of sensible objects change upon the senses. And when this is established, the statement of the dualists of the two [principles] is invalidated, and their ignorance of the aspects of wisdom in creating the harmful and the beneficial is established, for it may be that every harmful thing becomes beneficial in another state. And the statement of those from the Mu'tazilites who say: 'Every action that does not benefit another is not wisdom' is invalidated. Moreover, there is no harm at all except that it is possible for someone to benefit from it, either by way of indication, or by way of admonition, or by what is in it of reminding of blessing and warning of punishment, and by making known the One to whom belongs creation and command in creation, and other things that are numerous. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two groups: the dualists (الثنوية) who believe in two eternal principles (good and evil), and some Mu'tazilites who claim that any action that does not benefit another is not wise. Maturidi argues that harmful things can become beneficial in different circumstances, and all harm has some potential benefit—whether as an indication, admonition, reminder of blessings, or warning of punishment. Therefore, describing God's actions as foolish or unjust is invalid because humans cannot fully comprehend the wisdom behind them. The passage establishes that judgment based on limited human perception is flawed.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Harmful things in creation are pointless or unjust and cannot come from a wise God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that every harmful thing carries hidden benefits, such as lessons, reminders, or signs, so it is not devoid of wisdom.

Checkpoint

A) Because all actions benefit the doer directly.
B) Because even harmful things can be beneficial through indication, admonition, or reminding of blessings.
C) Because Mu'tazilites reject divine attributes.
D) Because wisdom is only known to prophets.

Key Takeaway
All creation, including harm, contains hidden wisdom; no true harm exists without some potential benefit.

?

?

The Two Bases for Foolishness in Human Actions
Maturidi enumerates the two bases for foolishness and negates them from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two criteria that make an action foolish in the witnessed realm and why these criteria cannot apply to Allah.

Core Idea
Foolishness arises from exceeding authority or opposing command; Allah is beyond both.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi establishes a foundational principle for understanding divine transcendence. He first identifies the two specific conditions that render an action foolish in human experience. By understanding these conditions, we can see why they are impossible for Allah, thus affirming His wisdom and justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two bases
The passage presents two criteria for foolishness. Find each one and notice how Maturidi defines them.
The two bases are: (1) transgressing ownership without permission, and (2) opposing command.
3
Reflect on why they don't apply to Allah
Ask yourself why these two bases are negated from Allah. Consider His absolute ownership and authority.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Note the affirmation of transcendence
The passage concludes that Allah is established as transcendent above any possibility of foolishness. Notice the affirmation.
This is a key doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل الذي يجعل الفعل في الشاهد سفها أحد أمرين : إما تعدى الملك لا بإذن من له الملك لذلك الفعل، أو لما فيه ركوب النهي ومخالفة الأمر ممن له الأمر والنهي؛ وكل ذلك الله جل ثناؤه منفي. ثبت أنه يتعالى عن احتمال لحوق هذا الوصف فعله . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وليس ذلك كالكذب، لأنه لا يصلح بحال [ أن يكون] كالفعل الذي ينقسم على الحكمة والسفه، والعدل والجور. وهذا من حيث الجملة لا انقلاب له، ومن حيث الوقوع في شيء على الإشارة إليه ممكن فيه الأمران باختلاف الأحوال والأسباب. لذلك لزم وصف الله تعالى في الجملة بالتعالي عن فعل السفه والجور وفي الإشارة أيضاً. لكن لا يجوز أن يوصف فيما ظهر فعله بالسفه والجور، بما لا يبلغه علم البشر ولا يدركه عقل ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the principle that makes an action foolish in the witnessed realm is one of two things: either transgressing ownership without permission of the owner of that action, or because it involves committing what is forbidden and opposing the command of the one who has command and prohibition; and all of that is negated from Allah, may His praise be exalted. It is established that He is transcendent above the possibility of this description attaching to His action. And there is no power except through Allah. And that is not like lying, because lying is not at all valid [to be] like an action that is divided into wisdom and foolishness, and justice and injustice. And this, in terms of generality, has no reversal, but in terms of occurrence in something by indication, both matters are possible in it due to differing states and causes. Therefore, it is necessary to describe Allah, exalted, in general as transcendent above the act of foolishness and injustice, and also in indication. However, it is not permissible to describe what appears of His action as foolishness and injustice, because human knowledge does not reach it nor does intellect perceive it. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. Maturidi identifies two bases for an action being foolish (سفه) in the witnessed world (الشاهد): (1) exceeding one's authority without permission, and (2) engaging in what is forbidden or opposing the one who has authority. Both are negated from God. Then he distinguishes lying (الكذب) from actions that can be wise or foolish; lying is never valid under any circumstance, unlike actions that may be wise or foolish depending on context. He then explains that in general, God's transcendence is fixed, but in specific instances that appear foolish to humans, we cannot judge because our knowledge is limited.

Key Terms
السفه
الملك
الأمر
النهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any action that appears harmful or unwise could be attributed to God.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that foolishness is defined by specific criteria that cannot apply to God, so apparent harm does not equate to foolishness.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the two bases for an action being foolish?
A) Ignorance and need.
B) Transgressing ownership without permission and opposing command/prohibition.
C) Harm and benefit.
D) Habit and nature.

Key Takeaway
Foolishness in actions is defined by two specific violations; since Allah is the absolute owner and commander, these cannot apply to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you act in a way that disobeys Allah, do you realize that you are committing the very basis of foolishness? How will you ensure your actions are not described by these two bases?
?
Reflect on an area of your life where you might be 'transgressing ownership' by using what Allah has given you in a way He did not permit. What will you change today?
?
The passage says foolishness involves opposing command. Are there commands of Allah that you are knowingly opposing? Ask yourself: am I willing to submit completely or do I cling to my own judgment?

Distinguishing Lying from Conditional Foolishness
Maturidi distinguishes lying from conditional actions and applies the rule to divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why lying is fundamentally different from actions that can be wise or foolish, and how this relates to describing Allah's actions.

Core Idea
Lying is always invalid, while other actions vary; God's transcendence is absolute in general and limited in specific.

Imam Maturidi now addresses a potential confusion: some might think that describing an action as foolish is like labeling it a lie. He explains that lying is categorically different because it can never be justified, unlike other actions which may be wise or foolish depending on context. This distinction helps us properly understand how to describe Allah's actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the contrast between lying and other actions.
2
Understand the distinction from lying
Maturidi says lying is not at all valid, while other actions can be wise or foolish. Understand why this distinction matters.
Lying is always evil; other actions are contextual.
3
General vs. specific description
The passage explains that in general, the description of foolishness is absolute, but in specific instances it depends on context.
4
Apply to Allah's actions
In general, we must describe Allah as transcendent over foolishness. But in specific actions that appear to us, we cannot call them foolish because our knowledge is limited.
This preserves God's transcendence while acknowledging human limitation.

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
