Rational Repulsiveness of Injustice and Foolishness
Maturidi argues that injustice, foolishness, and lying are inherently repugnant to reason and never become good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that injustice, foolishness, and lying are inherently and permanently repugnant to reason, and why this precludes attributing them to Allah.

Core Idea
Rational ugliness never becomes good, proving Allah transcends injustice.

Maturidi begins by asserting that the corruption of describing Allah with injustice, foolishness, and lying is known in two ways. The first way, which he details here, is that these qualities are inherently ugly in the intellect by both intuition and reflection. This ugliness is permanent and only intensifies with thought—unlike natural ugliness that can become familiar. From this, he draws a powerful consequence: if Allah could act unjustly, His promises and threats would be unreliable, which is impossible for the All-Knowing, Wise, Self-Sufficient. He then turns to critique the Mu'tazilite position, which, he argues, inadvertently implies need and lying in Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi structures his argument: he states the two ways, then elaborates the first way with an analogy, then applies it to Allah.
The passage contains both argument and polemic against the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the core analogy
Maturidi contrasts rational ugliness with natural ugliness. Find the example of taming wild animals. Ask: why is this analogy important?
The analogy shows that natural repulsiveness can change, but rational repulsiveness cannot.
3
Follow the consequence for Allah
After establishing the permanence of rational ugliness, Maturidi applies it to Allah: if He could do such acts, His promises and threats would be unreliable, which contradicts His attributes.
Note how he lists divine attributes: All-Knowing, Wise, Self-Sufficient.
4
Understand the critique of the Mu'tazila
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazilite view that things can occur against Allah's will leads to absurdities like lying and need in Allah. Identify the specific points he raises.
He uses their own logic to show it undermines divinity.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that Allah is exalted above such descriptions. Reflect on how this argument establishes divine transcendence.
This is a key Maturidi principle: Allah's actions are always wise and just.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم جملة ما به يعلم فساد الوصف بالجور والسفه والكذب وجهان. أحدهما قبح ذلك في العقول بالبديهة والفكر جميعا، حتى لا يزداد عند التأمل والبحث عنه إلا قبحا، ولا عند طول النظر فيه إلا فُحشا . وليس ذلك كالقبيح بالطبع، إن ذلك [قد] يصير حسنا بالاعتياد وطول الصحبة كالذبح وأنواع ذلك. وكذلك نجد جواهر الدواب والسباع والطيور مستوحشة عن الناس بالطباع نافرة عما يراد بها من أنواع المكاسب والأعمال؛ ثم تخرج عنها بالرياضة والتعليم حتى تألف بالذي كان تنفر عنه ، ويصير ذلك له كأنه الطباع المجبول. ولا يكون الذي قبح بالعقل بهذا الوصف أبدا، بل يزداد على طول النظر في شأنه. ثم على ذلك من احتمل فعله ذلك لا يوثق لوعده ولا يخاف وعيده، ولا يرغب في خيره ولا يؤمن شره. ذا شأنه وعمله فمحال احتمال إضافة مثله إلى العليم الحكيم بذاته الغنى بنفسه مع الوصف بأن لا يخفى عليه شيء، ولا يصعب عليه أمر فيها أراد بل على قول المعتزلة لا يؤمن منه هذا ، إذ قد تخرج أكثر الأشياء عن إرادته، ويوجد ما لا يريده في سلطانه فيه بلا سلطان له في الإخراج عنه إذ لم يرده ؛ ويريد زيادة سلطانه ويتولى ذلك أن يكون، فيُمنع عن نحو ما يريد أن يكون جميع خلقه مطيعين ويكون له في سلطانه وملكه الطاعة لا المعاصي فلا يكون . ثم قد كان وعد لقوم مُدَدًا لأعمارهم ، وهو المبقى لهم إليها، وكان في وعده أن يرزقهم إلى تلك المدد أنواع الرزق ويسوق إليهم أنواع [الخيرات ] ، فيأتي خلق من خلائقه فيقتلوهم قبل مضي المدة، فيمنعه عن إنجاز ما وُعِدُوا الوفاء بالفعل الذي أخبرهم أن يفعله من إبقاء حياتهم إلى تلك المدة. وفي ذلك إيجاب الحاجة ولحوق الكذب اللذين يحققان السفه والجور؛ مع تحقيقهم له القدرة على الظلم والجور والسفه والكذب وكل فعل لو كان لأسقط الربوبية وأزال الإلهية، فأدخلو إلهيته وربوبيته تحت القدرة والتدبير . فمتى يكون مع مثله أمن البقاء، حاله سكون القلب بالوفاء بالذي وعد ولا قوة إلا بالله مع ما كان موصوفا بالجود والكرم والعفو والإحسان، وفي الفعل بالوصف الذي ذكرنا زواله جل عن ذلك وتعالى.
Then the summary of that by which the corruption of describing [God] with injustice, foolishness, and lying is known has two aspects. One of them is the ugliness of that in intellects by both intuition and reflection, such that it does not increase upon contemplation and investigation except in ugliness, nor upon prolonged consideration of it except in obscenity. And that is not like what is ugly by nature, for that [may] become good by habit and long association, like slaughter and its kinds. And similarly we find the natures (جوهر) of beasts, predators, and birds are instinctively wild toward people, averse to what is intended from them of types of acquirements and works; then they come out of that by training and education until they become familiar with what they used to shrink from, and that becomes for them as if it were innate nature. But that which is ugly by intellect never has this description, rather it increases upon prolonged consideration of its state. Then upon that, whoever's action is such that one cannot trust his promise nor fear his threat, nor desire his good nor be safe from his evil—such is his state and action—it is impossible to attribute the like of it to the All-Knowing, Wise by His essence, Self-Sufficient, with the description that nothing is hidden from Him, nor is any matter difficult for Him when He wills. Rather, according to the Mu'tazilites, one is not safe from this from Him, since most things fall outside His will, and what He does not will exists in His dominion without His having authority to remove it because He did not will it; and He wills the increase of His dominion and undertakes that it should be, but is prevented from what He wills—that all His creatures be obedient and that in His dominion and kingdom there be obedience, not disobedience—but it does not happen. Then He had promised some people terms for their lifetimes, and He is the one who preserved them to reach them, and in His promise was to provide them up to those terms with types of provision and bring to them types of good things; then a creation from His creations comes and kills them before the term passes, preventing Him from fulfilling what they were promised, by the act He informed them He would do of preserving their lives until that term. In that is the imposition of need and the attachment of lying, both of which actualize foolishness and injustice; along with their affirming for Him the power to do injustice, foolishness, lying, and every act which, if done, would nullify lordship and remove divinity. So they have placed His divinity and lordship under power and management. When will there be, with the like of that, security of permanence, the state of the heart being tranquil with fulfillment of what He promised? And there is no power except through Allah, along with the fact that He is described with generosity, nobility, pardon, and beneficence, while by acting according to the description we mentioned, that disappears—He is exalted above that and transcendent.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is the first of two ways (وجهان) to know that describing God with injustice, foolishness, and lying is corrupt. Maturidi argues that these qualities are inherently ugly in the intellect (قبح في العقول) by both intuition (بديهة) and reflection (فكر), and this ugliness only increases with contemplation. This is unlike what is ugly by nature (القبيح بالطبع), which can become good through habit, like slaughter or taming wild animals. Rational ugliness never changes. Then Maturidi draws a consequence: if God could act foolishly, His promises and threats would be unreliable, which is impossible for the All-Knowing, Wise, Self-Sufficient God. He then critiques the Mu'tazilite position that things can occur against God's will, which would imply need and lying in God—impossible for one described with generosity and beneficence. This establishes God's transcendence above such attributes.

بديهة
فكر
عاد
كذب
وعد
وعيد
المعتزلة
ربوبية
إلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that what is rationally ugly could become good through habit, like some natural ugliness.
Correction: Maturidi stresses that rational ugliness is permanent and never becomes good, unlike natural ugliness which can change through habit.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how does the ugliness of injustice and foolishness in the intellect differ from what is ugly by nature?
A) Natural ugliness is stronger.
B) Intellectual ugliness increases with reflection, while natural ugliness can become good through habit.
C) Intellectual ugliness is only known by prophets.
D) Natural ugliness applies to divine actions.

Key Takeaway
Injustice, foolishness, and lying are inherently and permanently repugnant to reason, and cannot be attributed to Allah, who is All-Knowing, Wise, and Self-Sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a small lie or injustice by telling yourself it was for a greater good? How does Maturidi’s argument that rational ugliness never becomes good expose the weakness of that reasoning? What will you do the next time that justification tempts you?
?
If you truly believed that Allah’s promises are absolutely reliable because He cannot be unjust or foolish, how would that change the way you face uncertainty or fear about the future? Will you surrender that area of your life to Him today?
?
Consider a time when you doubted Allah’s wisdom because of a difficult situation. How does this lesson’s emphasis on His transcendence above foolishness reshape your response to such trials? What specific step can you take to strengthen your trust in His promise that He never wrongs anyone?

Second Way: Need and Ignorance Cannot Be Attributed to Allah
Maturidi argues that need and ignorance cause injustice and foolishness, which are impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why need and ignorance cannot be causes of any of Allah's actions, based on the ordered world demonstrating His self-sufficiency and knowledge.

Core Idea
Need and ignorance would nullify lordship; the world's order proves they are impossible for Allah.

Imam Maturidi continues his proofs against describing Allah with injustice, foolishness, or lying. The second way identifies the root causes of such actions—need and ignorance—and shows why they are absolutely impossible for the Creator. This short yet powerful argument connects the observable order of the universe to Allah's perfect attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic and its literal translation to grasp Maturidi's concise style. Pay attention to the key terms: حاجة (need) and جهل (ignorance).
Note the phrase 'والوجه الثاني' indicating this is the second of several arguments.
2
Understand the core argument
Identify that Maturidi states need and ignorance are what drive evil actions (injustice, foolishness). He then argues both are impossible for Allah because they would nullify His lordship (ربوبية) and management (تدبير).
This is a causal argument: if an action stems from need or ignorance, it cannot be attributed to Allah.
3
Reflect on the evidence from the world
Maturidi points to the existence of the world 'as it is'—ordered and with everything given its due—as evidence that its Owner is Self-Sufficient (غنى) and All-Knowing (علم). This directly refutes any possibility of need or ignorance in Allah.
The phrase 'بإعطاء كل شيء حقه' suggests perfect justice and wisdom in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني أن الذي يدعو إلى تلك الأفعال ويبعث عليها الحاجة والجهل. وقد ثبت الأمرين، إذ هما يسقطان الربوبية ويزيلان التدبير وفي وجود العالم على ما [هو] دلالة غنا صاحبه وعلمه بإعطاء كل شيء حقه دليل إحالة هذا الوصف، لذلك بطل أن يوصف في شيء من فعله بذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the second aspect is that which calls to those actions and urges them is need and ignorance. And both matters have been established, for they nullify lordship and remove management. And in the existence of the world as it is, there is an indication of the Self-Sufficiency of its Owner and His Knowledge, by giving everything its due, which is evidence of the impossibility of this description. Therefore, it is invalid that He be described in any of His actions with that. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is the second way (الوجه الثاني) to know the corruption of describing God with injustice, foolishness, and lying. Maturidi identifies the driving causes of such actions as need (حاجة) and ignorance (جهل). Both are impossible for God because they would nullify His lordship and management (تدبير). The existence of the world, with its order and giving everything its due, demonstrates God's Self-Sufficiency and Knowledge, proving that such negative descriptions cannot apply to His actions. Thus, it is invalid to describe any of God's actions with foolishness, injustice, or lying.

Key Terms
حاجة
جهل
ربوبية
تدبير
غنى
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Allah could be subject to need or ignorance, just as humans are.
Correction: Maturidi shows that need and ignorance would negate lordship and management, and the ordered world proves Allah's self-sufficiency and knowledge, making such attributes impossible for Him.

Checkpoint
According to the second way, what two causes drive actions like injustice and foolishness, and why are they impossible for God?
A) Power and will; they are impossible because God is all-powerful.
B) Need and ignorance; they nullify lordship and management, and the world's order shows God's self-sufficiency and knowledge.
C) Habit and nature; they change over time but God is eternal.
D) Desire and aversion; they imply imperfection in God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi’s second way proves that need and ignorance are impossible for Allah because they would contradict His lordship and the perfect order of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself acting out of a sense of personal need (e.g., needing approval, control, or security), how are you implicitly denying that Allah alone is Al-Ghani (Self-Sufficient)? What practical step can you take this week to consciously rely on His self-sufficiency instead of your own efforts?
?
In what areas of your life do you act as if you are ignorant of Allah's wise management, insisting on your own plans and judgments? How does this lesson's proof that Allah's knowledge and management are perfect challenge you to surrender a specific decision to Him today?
?
If you truly believe that Allah gives everything its due—as the world's order testifies—then where is the injustice you hold against Allah in your own life? What complaint about your circumstances are you unwilling to release, and how can you use this argument to uproot that doubt from your heart?

The Impossibility of Questioning God's Actions
Maturidi argues that God cannot be questioned because humans are fallible while God is not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God cannot be questioned because humans are inherently limited and fallible, while God is absolute and perfect.

Core Idea
God's actions are beyond human judgment because we are flawed and He is not.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a profound theological argument: since God is described by perfect attributes like knowledge and power for His very essence, and since every human, no matter how wise, is susceptible to foolishness and ignorance, it is impossible for humans to judge God's actions by their own standards. Maturidi explains why questioning God's actions is baseless and why the only proper response is humility and supplication. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full argument in Maturidi's own words. Pay attention to the flow from premises to conclusion.
The passage is dense; read it twice if needed.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice that Maturidi's central thesis is that God cannot be questioned about His actions. Find where he states this directly and where he supports it with the Quranic verse (21:23).
3
Understand the key comparison
Maturidi contrasts divine attributes (perfect, essential) with human attributes (contingent, susceptible to opposites). Grasp why this contrast undermines any human attempt to evaluate God's actions.
4
Consider the conclusion and practical implication
Maturidi concludes that since questioning God is impossible, one must instead submit and supplicate for guidance. Reflect on how this shapes the believer's attitude towards divine decrees.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذ كان الله جل ثناؤه موصوفًا بالعلم والقدرة والجبروت والحياة لذاته، لإحالة احتماله الأغيار، وإذ لم يوجد في الحكماء كذلك لم يجب تقديره في أفعاله من أفعال الحكماء في ظلم الشاهد وجملة هذا الأصل أنه لا حكيم في الشاهد إلا وهو محتمل للسفه، وكذلك الغنى والعليم والقدير محتمل لأضداد تلك الصفات، وكان بها موصوفا حتى أُكرم بأضدادها، فإنما له منها قدر ما أعطى منها. فهو متى رأى السفه في شيء بين أن يكون قد أُعطى صفته علم حقيقة الحكمة في ذلك أو لا، أو بلغ علمه ما يدرك حكمته أو لا، أو مما كان من القديمة باقية فيه يمنع ذلك إياه عن الإحاطة بذلك. فلذلك يبطل وجه دعوى العبد في فعل الله أن ذا ليس بحكمة ولا كذا. والذي يوضح ذلك علمه بجهله بأكثر الأشياء، وعلمه بحاجته وعجزه في أكثر الأمور، وإحاطته بسفهه في أغلب الأمر. ومن هذا وصفه في نفسه فخوضه فيما الله أن يفعله على الإشارة إليه دون لزوم الجملة - وليس اشترك فيها العقلاء، إذ ذلك حقيقة عمل العقل في الجملة وقد أعطى كل ذلك - عبث لا معنى له. وللذي بينا قال الله تعالى: لا يسأل عما يفعل وهم يسألون [الأنبياء، ٢٣/٢١]، إذ فعل كل أحد يحتمل السفه والحكمة، وفعله يجل عن السفه؛ وعلى كل أحد أمر ونهى إذ هو لغيره في الحقيقة، والله يتعالى عن ذلك؛ ولأن كلا إنما ملك قدرًا من الأشياء وجزءًا، والله المالك لها بكليتها ونحو ذلك مما يحيل معنى سؤال الرب. وإذا استحال ذلك فالجواب عنه تكلف. لكن الله بمنّه وفضله وعد الهداية لسبيله لمن جاهد فيه، فألزم ذلك الخضوع له والتضرع إليه ليطلعه على مكنون حكمته على قدر ما يتفضل به عليه بكرمه، فإنه على كل شيء قدير.
Then, since God, exalted be His praise, is described with knowledge, power, majesty, and life for His own essence, because it is impossible that He should be subject to change, and since [this] is not found in the wise [among humans] similarly, it is not necessary to measure His actions by the actions of the wise in the witness [of human experience]. The summary of this principle is that there is no wise person in the witness except that he is susceptible to foolishness, and likewise the rich, the knowledgeable, and the powerful are susceptible to the opposites of those attributes, and they are described by them until they are honored with their opposites. Therefore, he only has from them the amount that was given to him. So whenever he sees foolishness in a thing, it is clear whether he has been given the attribute of knowledge of the reality of wisdom in that matter or not, or his knowledge has reached what enables him to perceive its wisdom or not, or whether something from the previous state remains in him that prevents him from encompassing it. Therefore, the claim of the servant regarding God's action that this is not wisdom or such is invalidated. And what clarifies that is his knowledge of his ignorance of most things, his knowledge of his need and incapacity in most matters, and his awareness of his foolishness in most cases. One who is described in himself as such, his delving into what God does—by way of allusion to it without adhering to the general rule—and the rational ones do not participate in that, for that is in reality the work of reason in general, and he has been given all that—is futile and meaningless. And for what we have stated, God Almighty said: 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they will be questioned' [Quran 21:23], for the action of everyone is susceptible to foolishness and wisdom, but His action is exalted above foolishness; and upon everyone is command and prohibition, for he is [subject] to another in reality, and God is exalted above that; and because each only possesses a portion and a part of things, while God possesses them in their entirety, and similar things that render the meaning of questioning the Lord impossible. And when that is impossible, the answer about it is unnecessary. But God, by His grace and favor, promised guidance to His path for those who strive in it, so that necessitates humility before Him and supplication to Him so that He may reveal to them the hidden wisdom to the extent that He bestows upon them by His generosity, for He is over all things powerful.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a theological proof against the possibility of questioning God's actions. Maturidi begins by stating that God is described by attributes (knowledge, power, etc.) for His own essence, unlike humans who are subject to change and opposites. He then establishes that in the witnessed world (al-shahid), every 'wise' person is actually susceptible to foolishness (safah). Therefore, humans cannot use their own flawed standards to judge God's actions. The argument proceeds: whenever a human sees something that seems not wise, it is uncertain whether they have full knowledge or not, so their claim that God's act is unwise is invalid. The Quranic verse (21:23) is cited to confirm that God is not questioned because human actions are mixed with folly and wisdom, while God's actions are pure. The passage concludes that since it is impossible to question God, the only proper response is to submit and supplicate for guidance.

الحكمة

العبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can judge whether God's actions are wise or foolish based on their own understanding.
Correction: Maturidi argues that every human is potentially foolish and ignorant, so they cannot accurately assess divine wisdom. God's actions transcend human categories of wisdom and folly.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it impossible for humans to question God's actions?
A) Because God has no attributes that can be understood.
B) Because humans are inherently susceptible to foolishness and lack full knowledge of wisdom.
C) Because questioning God is a sin.
D) Because the Quran forbids asking questions.

Key Takeaway
God's perfect and essential attributes render His actions beyond human scrutiny; the proper response is humility and supplication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you recall a moment in your life when you felt that a certain trial or outcome was 'unwise' from your perspective? Knowing now that your judgment is inherently limited, what will you tell yourself when such thoughts arise again?
?
The passage says humans are 'ignorant of most things' and 'in need and incapacity'. Does your daily life reflect this humility, or do you often act as if you have full understanding? What practical step can you take today to acknowledge your dependence on God's wisdom?
?
You cannot question God, but you can supplicate. Is there a situation in your life where you have been asking 'Why, O Allah?' rather than turning to Him in humble request? Commit now to transform that ‘why’ into a ‘please’ — what will you specifically ask for?

Shared Ownership of Actions Does Not Imply Shirk
Maturidi argues that attributing actions to both God and humans does not constitute shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing the same action to both God and humans does not amount to shirk, according to Maturidi's reasoning.

Core Idea
Co-ownership of actions does not require co-divinity.

Maturidi addresses a common objection: if an action is attributed to both God and humans, doesn't that imply multiple gods? He argues that ownership and agency can be shared in different aspects without implying partnership in divinity. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own rhythm come through.
2
Identify the principle about ownership
Notice how Maturidi begins by stating that actions are owned by both God and humans, yet this does not necessitate shirk in ownership.
He uses the term 'ملك' (ownership) for both.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the examples: feeding, clothing, and provision are attributed to both God and creation without implying shirk. This supports his principle.
These are everyday actions.
4
Understand the aspect argument
Maturidi clarifies that 'aspects of the action' differ for each agent, so no single aspect is shared in a way that mixes knowledge and ignorance.
Each aspect encompasses the whole from its perspective.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
He concludes that if partnership in worldly matters (property, trade) is recognized, why not in divine matters? This refutes the Mu'tazila claim.
The phrase 'no power except with God' ends the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الفعل نفسه يجعلونه الله ملكا وكذلك للعبد وكذا كل ملك لأحد فهو الله ملك وللعبد كذلك ولم يوجب ذلك شركا بينهما في ملك الأفعال والأعيان فكيف أوجب فيما نحن فيه شركا ثم يضاف إلى الله الإطعام والكسوة والرزق وذلك بعينه يضاف إلى الخلق ولا يوجب شركا فمثله الذي نحن فيه مع ما بينا جهات الفعل ثم لم نقل الفعل نفسه من تلك الجهات مشترك إذ كل جهة تحيط بالكل وكذلك من يعلم الفعل من وجه ويجهله من وجه لم نقل أشرك جهله علمه فما بالهم يزعمون أن ذا شركة معقولة بل لو كان ثمة عقل لكان يكون ذا كذبا معقولا ولا قوة إلا بالله وكل هذه الوجوه على قول من يقول بخلق الشيء غيره ويعلم أيضا فساد دعوى المعتزلة ثم يقال له قد يقال في الشرك في قرية على تفرق الأملاك وفي التجارة على تفرق المعاملات فقل بين الله وبين الخلق شرك في العالم ثم في الأفعال بما كان منه أمر وإقدار ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the principle is that they make the action itself a possession of God, and likewise for the servant, and similarly every possession of anyone is a possession of God and also for the servant. And that did not necessitate shirk between them in the ownership of actions and entities. So how did it necessitate in what we are discussing shirk? Then feeding, clothing, and provision are attributed to God, and the very same are attributed to creation and do not necessitate shirk. So the like of that is what we are discussing, along with what we have clarified about the aspects of the action. Then we did not say that the action itself from those aspects is shared, because each aspect encompasses the whole. Similarly, one who knows the action from one aspect and is ignorant of it from another aspect, we did not say that his ignorance associates with his knowledge. So what is wrong with them that they claim this is an intelligible sharing? Nay, if there were intellect, this would be an intelligible lie. And there is no power except with God. And all these aspects are according to the saying of whoever says that the creation of a thing is other than it, and the corruption of the Mu'tazila claim is also known. Then it is said to him: It may be said regarding partnership in a village due to the division of properties, and in trade due to the division of transactions. So say: between God and creation there is partnership in the world, then in actions, due to His command and enabling. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that attributing actions to both God and humans implies shirk. Maturidi begins by stating the principle that actions are owned by God and also by humans, yet this does not necessitate shirk in ownership. He gives examples: feeding, clothing, and provision are attributed to God and to creation without implying shirk. He clarifies that aspects of an action differ, and no single aspect is shared in a way that mixes knowledge and ignorance. He then argues that if the Mu'tazila used reason, they would see that their claim is an 'intelligible lie'. He concludes that the same reasoning applies to the attribution of creation of things and actions, and that partnership in worldly matters (like property or trade) is recognized, so why not in divine matters?

Key Terms
شرك
ملك
جهات الفعل
خلق الشيء غيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If both God and humans are described as 'actors' or 'owners' of an action, that implies two equal agents.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the aspects of agency differ, so there is no true sharing that implies partnership in divinity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does attributing the same action to both God and humans not constitute shirk?
A) Because the action is entirely God's and humans only acquire it
B) Because the aspects of the action are different for each agent, so there is no true sharing
C) Because humans do not actually perform actions
D) Because God's ownership is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that attributing actions to both God and humans is not shirk because the aspects of agency differ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever feared that acknowledging God's role in your actions would diminish your responsibility? Reflect on a mistake you made. How can you hold yourself accountable while still believing that God created that action and enabled you? What would change in your repentance?
?
The lesson uses the example of feeding, clothing, and provision. Think of a provision you received recently—like food or a job. Did you thank God and also recognize the human means? How can you practice this dual attribution daily without falling into either ingratitude or self-reliance?

Refuting the Naming Objection
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite naming objection by distinguishing aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite objection based on names like 'obedient' and 'submissive', by distinguishing between aspects of attribution.

Core Idea
Names like 'obedient' do not create contradiction because the aspect differs.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God is named 'obedient' and 'submissive' for creating obedience in humans, then humans must also be creators. Maturidi responds by clarifying that the aspect of attribution differs: God is the creator of the act, while humans are the acquirers. He points out that the Mu'tazila themselves call God the creator of movements but do not name Him by those movements, showing inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. This is Maturidi's response to the naming objection.
Notice the phrase 'اختلاف الجهة' which is key.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite argument
The Mu'tazila claimed that if God is called 'obedient' because He creates obedience, then humans who also create actions should be called 'obedient' in the same sense.
3
Identify Maturidi's response
Maturidi says the aspect differs: God's relationship to the act is as creator, while the human's is as acquirer. The name applies each according to its own aspect.
He also notes that the Mu'tazila themselves do not name God by the actions He creates, like 'mover' or 'corrupter'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
This shows that the naming objection does not hold because names are not univocal across God and humans.
The lesson ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' as a pious statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما احتجاجهم بالتسمية من المطيع والخاضع ونحو ذلك فقد بينا اختلاف الجهة على أحد القولين والفعل على الآخر وإنما سمي كل بالذي له على ما بينا الجهات على أنهم جعلوه خالقا للحركات ولفساد الأشياء غير مسمى به لأنه خلق فمثله الأفعال ولا قوة إلا بالله ثم عارض فعلا واحدا لفاعلين بقول واحد وخبر واحد قال الشيخ رحمه الله يجوزان في الشاهد قد يقال هذا قول جماعة وخبر المتواتر وهو قول فلان وفلان وخبر فلان وفلان فلئن كان ذا أصله فيجب به جواز الآخر إذ به يلزم الآخر ولو كان كل ما يجوز في الشاهد هو دليل الغائب ليجب التفريق بين الفعل والقول في الغائب كما وجب في الشاهد وهذا يبين وهمه
As for their argument by naming, from the obedient and the submissive and the like, we have clarified the difference of aspect according to one of the two sayings and the action according to the other. And each is only named by what belongs to him according to what we clarified regarding aspects. Despite that, they made Him the creator of movements and the corruption of things, yet He is not named by that because it is creation, and similarly for actions. And there is no power except with God. Then he countered one act of two agents with one statement and one report. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: In the witnessed world it is permissible that it may be said: this is the statement of a group and a mutawatir report, and it is the statement of so-and-so and so-and-so, and the report of so-and-so and so-and-so. If this is its principle, then the permissibility of the other must follow, because by it the other is entailed. And if everything that is permissible in the witnessed world is an evidence for the unseen, then it would be necessary to differentiate between action and speech in the unseen as was necessitated in the witnessed world. And this shows his error.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of Mu'tazilite arguments. The first part addresses their objection based on names like 'obedient' and 'submissive'—the Mu'tazila argued that since these names imply a direct relationship to the action, attributing them to both God and humans would be contradictory. Maturidi responds that the aspect differs: the action is attributed differently. He notes that the Mu'tazila themselves call God the creator of movements and corruption without naming Him by those actions (e.g., 'mover' or 'corrupter'), so the same applies to actions. Then he addresses the objection that one action cannot have two agents, using a counterexample from the visible world: a single statement can be attributed to a group (like a mutawatir report) and also to individuals within that group. The Shaykh (Maturidi) says that in the witnessed world, it is permissible to say 'this is the statement of a group' and also 'the statement of so-and-so'. If this principle is accepted for speech, then it should apply to actions as well. He points out that if one insists that every permissible thing in the witnessed world must have a parallel in the unseen, then they would have to differentiate between action and speech in the unseen as they do in the witnessed—but they do not, showing their inconsistency.

Key Terms
تسمية
مطيع
خاضع
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is called 'obedient' because He creates obedience, then humans should also be called 'obedient' in the same sense, leading to contradiction.
Correction: The aspect of attribution differs: God is the creator, humans are the acquirers. The name applies each according to its proper aspect.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi cite as evidence that the Mu'tazila are inconsistent in their naming?
A) They call God 'obedient' and 'submissive'
B) They call God the creator of movements and corruption but do not name Him accordingly
C) They deny that God creates actions
D) They affirm free will

Key Takeaway
Names like 'obedient' do not create a contradiction because the aspect of attribution differs between God and humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of obedience, do you truly feel that it is entirely from Allah's creation? How would your humility change if you recognized that even your ability to obey is His gift, not your own?
?
Have you ever looked down on someone else's good deed because they are sinful? How does this lesson's distinction between Creator and performer challenge you to see Allah's mercy even in flawed vessels?
?
If God is never called 'obedient' despite creating obedience, what does that say about your own identity? Are you defined by your deeds, or is there a deeper aspect to who you are before Him?

The One-Act/Two-Agents Analogy from Speech
Maturidi counters the one-act/two-agents objection with the example of a single statement by a group.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the example of a single statement attributed to a group and to individuals to refute the claim that one action cannot have two agents.

Core Idea
A single statement can be attributed to both a group and individuals, so actions can too.

The Mu'tazila argued that one action cannot have two agents, which would make human action and divine creation incompatible. Maturidi counters with an example from the witnessed world: a mutawatir report is a single statement attributed to the entire group of transmitters, yet it is also attributed to each individual. He argues that if this is acceptable for speech, it should be acceptable for actions. He then exposes the inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
This passage presents Maturidi's counterexample to the one-act/two-agents objection.
Focus on the example of a mutawatir report and the Shaykh's reasoning.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
They claimed that a single act cannot be attributed to two agents, so if God creates the act, human agent is not the agent.
3
Analyze the counterexample
Maturidi says that in the witnessed world, a single statement can be attributed to a group (e.g., 'this is the statement of the group') and also to individuals ('the statement of so-and-so'). This is accepted without contradiction.
The example of mutawatir report is key: it is the statement of the whole group and of each individual.
4
Expose the inconsistency
If the Mu'tazila insist that every permissible thing in the witnessed world must apply analogically to the unseen, then they should differentiate between action and speech in the unseen as they do in the witnessed. But they don't, showing their error.
Maturidi concludes 'وهذا يبين وهمه' (this shows his error).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for their argument by naming, from the obedient and the submissive and the like, we have clarified the difference of aspect according to one of the two sayings and the action according to the other. And each is only named by what belongs to him according to what we clarified regarding aspects. Despite that, they made Him the creator of movements and the corruption of things, yet He is not named by that because it is creation, and similarly for actions. And if everything that is permissible in the witnessed world is an evidence for the unseen, then it would be necessary to differentiate between action and speech in the unseen as was necessitated in the witnessed world. And this shows his error.

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of Mu'tazilite arguments. The first part addresses their objection based on names like 'obedient' and 'submissive'—the Mu'tazila argued that since these names imply a direct relationship to the action, attributing them to both God and humans would be contradictory. Maturidi responds that the aspect differs: the action is attributed differently. He notes that the Mu'tazila themselves call God the creator of movements and corruption without naming Him by those actions (e.g., 'mover' or 'corrupter'), so the same applies to actions. Then he addresses the objection that one action cannot have two agents, using a counterexample from the visible world: a single statement can be attributed to a group (like a mutawatir report) and also to individuals within that group. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A single action cannot have two agents; therefore, if God creates an act, the human cannot be an agent.
Correction: A single statement can have multiple agents (group and individuals), so actions can also have multiple agents from different aspects.

D) A single act of writing by two people

Key Takeaway
The witnessed-world example of a single statement with multiple speakers shows that one action can have two agents without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about Allah's actions, do you test it for consistency with the principles you accept in everyday life? Reflect on a time you might have been inconsistent without realizing it. How can you develop the intellectual honesty Maturidi models?
?
The Mu'tazilites insisted on strict analogy but failed to apply it uniformly. Are there areas in your faith practice where you demand logical consistency from others but allow exceptions for yourself? Commit to examining one such area today.
?
Maturidi uses a simple example from speech to refute a complex theological claim. What everyday experiences could you reflect on to deepen your understanding of divine transcendence? Choose one and ponder how it might reveal a truth about Allah's actions.

Maturidi's Dismissal of Al-Ka'bi's Analogy
Maturidi dismisses al-Ka'bi's analogy about lying and truth as absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers al-Ka'bi's analogy about lying and truth to be foolish and how it reflects the principle of divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Divine actions are not subject to human moral analogies.

Al-Ka'bi attempted to argue that if God's actions differ from those of creatures, then what is lying from a creature could be truth from God. Maturidi finds this so absurd that he does not even bother to refute it fully, merely expressing bewilderment at how such an idea could arise. This lesson explores the underlying issue of divine transcendence (tanzih) and why analogical reasoning fails when applied to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to al-Ka'bi's claim and Maturidi's reaction.
Notice the abrupt dismissal: 'I do not know what pushed him to this imagination.'
2
Understand al-Ka'bi's argument
Al-Ka'bi says: if we allow differentiation between God and others, then lying from a creature could be truth from God. What is he assuming about how attributes relate?
He assumes that attributes like 'lying' and 'truth' apply univocally across beings.
3
Analyze Maturidi's response
Maturidi dismisses the argument as foolish. He does not give a detailed refutation. Why? Because the premise is so obviously flawed from the perspective of tanzih.
Maturidi's bewilderment signals that the analogy is not worth serious consideration.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Reflect on why the analogy fails: human moral categories are not directly transferable to Allah. Divine actions are qualitatively different. This is a key point in Islamic theology about tanzih.
This lesson guards against anthropomorphic thinking about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر [الكعبي] معنى يدل على سفهه، فقال: لو جاز التفريق بين الله وبين ما يكون من غيره لجاز أن يكون الكذب من غيره يكون منه صدقاً. فلا أدري أي شيء دفعه إلى هذا الخيال؛
Then al-Ka'bi mentioned a meaning indicating his foolishness, and said: 'If it were permissible to differentiate between God and what comes from other than Him, then it would be permissible for lying from other than Him to be truth from Him.' I do not know what pushed him to this imagination.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ka'bi's objection: if we allow that God's actions are different from those of His creatures, then in principle what is lying from a creature could be truth from God. Maturidi dismisses this as foolishness and does not even bother to refute it fully, merely expressing bewilderment at how al-Ka'bi arrived at such an imagination. The point is that divine transcendence (tanzih) allows attributes to be understood analogically, not univocally.

Key Terms
تفريق
كذب
صدق
خيال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God's actions differ from creatures, then moral categories become relative or interchangeable (e.g., lying could be truth from God).
Correction: Maturidi shows this is foolish because divine actions transcend human categories; they are not subject to the same moral framework. Tanzih means God is incomparable, not that attributes invert.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi respond to al-Ka'bi's analogy about lying and truth?
A) He provides a detailed rebuttal.
B) He dismisses it as foolish and expresses bewilderment.
C) He agrees with the analogy.
D) He reinterprets lying as truth.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi dismisses al-Ka'bi's analogy as foolish because it fails to recognize that God's attributes are not univocally comparable to human actions.

?
Maturidi dismisses the argument as 'foolish imagination.' Imagine standing before Allah and realizing you have been making analogies about Him based on human experience. What would you feel? How can you guard against that from now on?

Maturidi's Refutation of the Enemy's Claim of Favor
Maturidi argues that empowering an enemy to disbelieve contradicts divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that enabling an enemy to disbelieve would allow the enemy to claim a favor upon God, thereby making divine punishment unjust and contradicting wisdom.

Core Idea
Enabling an enemy to disobey lets him claim a favor on God.

Maturidi presents a striking thought experiment. If God were to give an enemy (like Pharaoh) the power to nullify His lordship but the enemy refrains, the enemy could argue that his restraint preserves God's attributes. This would make punishment seem arbitrary and unjust, thus proving that God does not grant such power without wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage slowly, noting the structure. First, Maturidi states the principle; then he imagines the enemy's speech.
2
Identify the enemy's claim
Find where the enemy says 'I have every favor upon you.' Understand why he says this.
The enemy claims credit for not using the power to break God's lordship.
3
Understand the logical contradiction
Reflect on why Maturidi says this is outside the bounds of wisdom. How does this argument refute the Mu'tazilī position?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أنه إذا أخبر أنه لا يؤمن بالله، وقد علم ذلك، وهو عدوه، وإقدار العدو على تسفيه المقدر وتقويته على نقض ملكه وإبطال ربوبيته خارج عن حد الحكمة ببديهة العقل. مع ما فيه تمكين عدوه لأعظم مِئَةٍ له عليه أن يقول : لي عليك كل منة، إذ ملكتني نقض ربوبتك، بما لا يكون رب جاهل ، وقوّيتني على إزالة حكمتك، بما لا يكون حكيم كذوب وقد جعلت لي القدرة على ذلك، وبذلك أمرتني ، وقد تعلم أني لو شئت لفعلت؛ فتمت لك الربوبية وسلمت لك الحكمة . فمِنَّتي عليك أعظم ونعمتي لديك أعم. فبأي نعمة لك تعاقبني ، وبأي حكمة تأمرني ، وبي تمت لك؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The second: that when He informs that he will not believe in God, and He knows that, and he is His enemy — and enabling the enemy to trivialize the ordained and strengthen him to break His dominion and nullify His lordship is outside the bounds of wisdom by the intuition of reason. Along with what it contains of empowering His enemy to say, 'I have every favor upon you, since you gave me power to break your lordship, by what would not be a Lord who is ignorant, and you strengthened me to remove your wisdom, by what would not be a wise liar; and you gave me power over that, and by that you commanded me, and you know that if I wished I would have done it; so your lordship is complete and your wisdom is intact. So my favor upon you is greater and my grace before you is more comprehensive. So by which of your blessings do you punish me, and by which wisdom do you command me, and by me is your [lordship] completed?' And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents the second point of Maturidi's refutation. He argues that if God gives an enemy (like Pharaoh) the power to disbelieve, and then punishes him for it, this would be outside the bounds of wisdom. The reason is that the enemy could argue: 'You gave me power to nullify Your lordship, but I did not use it — so I have a favor upon You. You also know that if I wished, I could have done so. Therefore, Your lordship and wisdom remain intact only because of my restraint.' This would make God's punishment seem unjust because the enemy's restraint is the reason God's attributes remain perfect. The argument shows the absurdity of the Mu'tazilī claim that God gives power to disbelieve while also commanding belief.

Key Terms

ربوبية
حكمة
منة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God giving power to do evil does not affect His justice.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such power would allow the creature to claim a favor for not using it, making punishment unjust.

Checkpoint

A) That God is unjust.
B) That he has a favor upon God by not using his power to nullify God's lordship.
C) That God has no wisdom.
D) That God is not all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that enabling an enemy to disbelieve would allow the enemy to claim a favor for not using that power, making punishment unjust and contradicting wisdom.

?

?

Why Evil Cannot Be Attributed to God Even as Creation
Refutation of attributing evil to God from creation perspective

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing evil or kufr to God is impermissible even from the perspective of creation, based on the linguistic principle that 'from God' implies command or grace.

Core Idea
Evil cannot be attributed to God because 'from God' means command or grace.

This lesson tackles a subtle objection: some admit that God does not command evil, but claim He creates it, so evil can be attributed to Him as Creator. Imam Maturidi refutes this by examining the meaning of the phrase 'from God' and showing it is reserved for command and blessing, not for base things. By the end, you will see how careful theological language protects divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the opening objection: the opponent concedes kufr is not from God by command, but claims it is from Him by creation. Notice how they try to separate command and creation to justify attribution.
This objection is subtle, so pay attention to the distinction.
2
See the immediate rejection
The Shaykh rejects the claim outright, listing numerous vile things (evil, misguidance, Iblis, Satan, filth) that no one would attribute to God. This shows the absurdity of the attribution, even from creation.
The force of the rejection is in the examples.
3
Learn the linguistic and theological basis
The Shaykh explains why: the phrase 'from God' idiomatically implies either a command or a bestowal of grace. Since evil involves neither, attribution is impermissible. Note that God is truly the Creator of all, but we do not ascribe contemptible things to Him.
This principle protects divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال: فإن قيل: لا نقول: الكفر من الله من جهة الأمر، ولكن نقول من جهة الخلق. قال: الأمر دون الفعل. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: فنقول: لا نقول: «الكفر من الله» طريق، ولا «الشر» من الله؛ كذلك لا نقول: الضلال من الله؛ وكذلك لا أحد يقول: إبليس من الله، أو الشيطان من الله، أو كل قذر ونتن من الله، أو كل فساد من الله. ثبت أن هذا اللفظ فاسد فيما كانت الخلق أيضاً. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والأصل في ذلك أن القول منه يخرج مخرج دعوى الأمر أو إضافة الإنعام، وليس في ذلك واحد منهما ألبتة، فلا يجوز الإضافة إليه. وهو كما قلنا إن الله في التحقيق وإن رب كل شيء وإله كل شيء وخالق كل شيء، وكل شيء له، لا يقال ذلك في الأوراث والخبائث والشيطان ونحو ذلك من الأشياء التي لا تذكر أنفسها إلا على الاستخفاف بها، فإضافتها إلى الواحد يخرج على ذلك، وإن كانت في أنها مخلوقة كغيرها مما يضاف إلى الله، فمثله الذي نحن فيه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he said: If it is said: We do not say that kufr is from God from the perspective of command, but we say from the perspective of creation. He said: Command is less than action. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: We say: We do not say 'kufr is from God' in any way, nor 'evil is from God'; likewise we do not say 'misguidance is from God'; and likewise no one says 'Iblis is from God', or 'Satan is from God', or every filth and impurity is from God, or every corruption is from God. It is established that this expression is invalid, even from the perspective of creation. And there is no power except in God. And the basis in that is that the statement 'from Him' takes the form of claiming command or adding bestowal of grace, and there is not in that one of them at all, so attribution to Him is not permissible. And it is as we said: God, in reality, is the Lord of everything, the God of everything, the Creator of everything, and everything belongs to Him, but it is not said that in the case of impurities, foul things, Satan, and similar things that are not mentioned except with contempt, so attributing them to the One is exceptional in that, even though they are created like other things that are attributed to God. So the like is that which we are discussing. And there is no power except in God.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is the final refutation of a nuanced objection. An opponent concedes that kufr is not from God by command, but claims it is from God by creation (khalq). The Shaykh rejects this categorically: the expression 'kufr from God' is simply not said in any way, nor any vile thing like evil, misguidance, Iblis, Satan, filth. The phrase 'from God' (min Allah) in Arabic idiomatically implies either a command (amr) or a bestowal of grace (in'am). Since neither applies to these base things, attribution is impermissible. Although God is truly the Lord, God, and Creator of everything, and everything belongs to Him, one does not attribute contemptible things to Him because they are only mentioned with disdain. The passage upholds divine transcendence (tanzih) and proper theological language: even if God creates all, we do not ascribe evil directly to Him.

Key Terms
الكفر من الله
جهة الخلق
القول منه
إضافة الإنعام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since God creates everything, evil can be attributed to Him as Creator.
Correction: The phrase 'from God' linguistically implies command or grace, so it cannot be used for evil, even though God creates all.

Checkpoint
According to the Shaykh, why can we not say 'evil is from God' even if God creates it?
A) Because creation is not from God
B) Because the phrase 'from God' implies command or grace
C) Because evil does not exist
D) Because humans create their own evil

Key Takeaway
Even though God creates all things, we must never attribute evil or contemptible things to Him because 'from God' implies command or grace, not vileness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even for a moment, blamed God for something bad that happened to you? How does this lesson challenge that reaction, and what commitment can you make to guard your tongue and heart from such attribution?

The Curse (La'n) and the Verse of False Accusation
Maturidi refutes the Khariji view on curse using the verse of qadhf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Khariji claim that every major sinner deserves God's curse, and his interpretation of the verse on false accusation.

Core Idea
Not every curse in the Quran means actual damnation; context and meaning matter.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi responds to an extreme view held by the Kharijites, who believed that any major sin incurs an eternal curse from Allah. Using the verse about the slander of Aisha (the incident of Ifk), Maturidi carefully distinguishes between the literal meaning of la'n (expulsion) and its theological application. This lesson unpacks his nuanced argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the verse
The verse 'Those are the liars to Allah' was revealed about a specific incident (the slander of Aisha), not as a general rule for all false accusers. Maturidi begins by limiting its application.
This is key to his refutation.
2
Analyze the definition of la'n
Maturidi states that la'n means expulsion (tard). He then argues that not every expelled person is expelled for a sin, and the verse's mention of la'n is not a literal occurrence but a deterrent.
Read the Arabic carefully: 'والأصل أن اللعن هو الطرد'.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical turning
Maturidi turns the argument on its head: if anyone deserved the curse, it would be the one who holds the extreme view, because according to his own doctrine, his sins warrant it. This shows the inconsistency of the Khariji position.
Consider the implications for the Sunni position on major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقوله : فأولئك عند الله هم الكاذبون ، ولا كل قاذف كذلك. وجملة ذلك أن من اجترأ على ذلك القول واستخف بمقت الله ولعنه حلت به والأصل أن اللعن هو الطرد. ولا كل مطرود بارتكاب مأثم، ولا يقال [فيمن] لو عُذب قدر ما استوجب؛ ولا كل من يقال [فيه] عليه لعنة الله يستحقها. ولو كان أحد يستحقها كان أحق بها صاحب هذا القول، إذ هو معلوم أنه كان يتعاطى الفسق ويختلف إلى الأئمة الجائرة، وكل ذلك على مذهبه يوجب اللعن حقيقة ، وما في الآية [من] قول اللعن لا حقيقة الوقوع . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
He said: "Those are the liars to Allah." But not every false accuser is like that. The sum of that is that whoever dares to say that and belittles Allah's wrath and curse, the curse befalls him. And the origin of la'n is expulsion. Not every expelled one is [expelled] for committing a sin, and it is not said concerning one [who] if he were punished as much as he deserved... nor does everyone of whom it is said "the curse of Allah be upon him" actually deserve it. And if anyone deserved it, the one who holds this saying would be more deserving of it, for it is known that he used to engage in sin and frequent unjust rulers. All of that according to his own doctrine necessarily requires the la'n in reality, but in the verse the mention of la'n is not actual occurrence. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Khariji claim that every major sinner deserves eternal curse and expulsion from faith. Maturidi explains that the verse "Those are the liars to Allah" refers to a specific incident (Ifk) and does not apply to every false accuser. He argues that la'n means expulsion (tard), but not every sinner expelled from a state is actually cursed by God. The one who holds the opposing view (the Khariji) is himself engaged in sin and thus is more deserving of the curse. The verse's mention of la'n is a deterrent, not a literal occurrence. Thus, Maturidi defends the Sunni position that major sins do not entail eternal curse or removal from faith.

Key Terms
اللعن
الطرد
القذف
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every major sinner deserves God's eternal curse and expulsion from faith.
Correction: Maturidi shows that la'n means expulsion, but not every sinner is expelled; the Quranic curse in the verse is specific and not literal.

Checkpoint

A) Punishment
B) Expulsion
C) Disbelief
D) Anger

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the curse in the verse is not a literal punishment for all sinners but a specific warning, and that the extreme view of the Kharijites is self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Khariji thought that a single major sin could lead to eternal curse. Maturidi showed that not every mention of curse in the Quran is literal. Reflect on a time you were quick to judge someone’s eternal fate based on a sin they committed. How can you change that attitude starting today?
?
Maturidi points out that the Khariji themselves engaged in sins according to their own doctrine, so they would be more deserving of curse. ?

The Ash'ari Middle Path on Seeing Allah
Ibn Asakir reports the Ash'ari position on ru'ya as a middle way between anthropomorphism and denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari's position on the vision of Allah navigates between anthropomorphism and outright denial, affirming vision while preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Affirming vision of Allah without modality or limitation is the Ash'ari middle way.

The question of whether believers will see Allah in the afterlife divided early Muslim theologians. Some fell into anthropomorphism, claiming Allah is seen as a physical object. Others denied the possibility entirely. Imam al-Ash'ari charted a middle path that upheld the reality of vision while safeguarding Allah's transcendence. In this passage, Ibn Asakir reports this balanced position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three groups
Read the passage and identify the three theological positions mentioned: the Hashwiyya anthropomorphists, the Mu'tazila and others who deny vision, and al-Ash'ari's middle path.
Each group has a distinct view on whether Allah can be seen and how.
2
Understand the extremes
Examine what the Hashwiyya claimed (Allah seen as limited and qualified) and what the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Najjariyya claimed (Allah is never seen). Note the problems with each extreme.
3
Focus on the Ash'ari middle position
Al-Ash'ari affirms vision but negates three things: hulul (indwelling), hudud (limits), and takyif (modality). Understand what each negation means for divine transcendence.
4
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the analogy: just as Allah sees us without being limited or qualified, so we will see Him. How does this analogy preserve transcendence while affirming vision?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتوسط الأشعري في رؤية الباري، فيقول ابن عساكر: وكذلك قالت الحشوية المشبهة: إن الله سبحانه وتعالى يرى مكيفاً محدوداً كسائر المرئيات. وقالت المعتزلة والجهمية والنجارية: إنه سبحانه لا يرى بحال من الأحوال. فسلك الأشعري طريقة بينهما فقال: يرى من غير حلول ولا حدود ولا تكييف، كما يرانا هو سبحانه وتعالى وهو غير محدود ولا مكيف، فكذلك نراه وهو غير محدود ولا مكيف.
And al-Ash'ari takes a middle position on the vision of the Creator. Ibn Asakir says: Likewise, the Hashwiyya anthropomorphists said: Indeed, God the Glorious and Exalted is seen as qualified and limited like other visible things. And the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Najjariyya said: Indeed, He the Glorious is not seen under any circumstance. So al-Ash'ari took a path between the two and said: He is seen without indwelling, without limits, and without qualification, just as He sees us, the Glorious and Exalted, while He is not limited nor qualified. Thus we see Him, while He is not limited nor qualified.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage discusses the theological position on seeing Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife. It presents al-Ash'ari's view as a middle path between two extremes. The Hashwiyya and anthropomorphists (musabbiha) claimed that Allah will be seen as a physical object, with modality and limitation. The Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Najjariyya denied the possibility of seeing Allah altogether. Al-Ash'ari's intermediate position affirms that Allah will indeed be seen, but without hulul (indwelling in a place), without hudud (limits), and without takyif (modality). He draws an analogy: just as Allah sees us without being limited or qualified, so we will see Him without those attributes. This preserves the reality of vision while safeguarding divine transcendence.

Key Terms
رؤية الباري
الحشوية المشبهة
المعتزلة
الجهمية
النجارية
حلول
حدود
تكييف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'ari's view on ru'ya implies anthropomorphism.
Correction: In fact, it denies all modality and limitation, preserving divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
Which does al-Ash'ari affirm regarding the vision of Allah?
A) Allah will be seen with physical form.
B) Allah will not be seen at all.
C) Allah will be seen without indwelling, limits, or modality.
D) Allah will be seen only in dreams.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari affirms that Allah will be seen in the afterlife, but without any indwelling, limits, or modality, preserving His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine standing before Allah and seeing Him, do you unconsciously picture a physical form or limited being? How will you retrain your heart to anticipate that vision without any modality or limit, as the Ash'ari middle path teaches?
?
If a friend told you that it is impossible to see Allah because He is transcendent, could you articulate the Ash'ari response from this passage? Are you prepared to defend the reality of ru'ya without falling into anthropomorphism?
?
The Ash'ari analogy says we will see Him as He sees us—without limitation. Do you truly live with the certainty that you will see Allah? How would that certainty change how you prioritize your life today?

The Middle Path: Ash'ari's Position on Ru'ya
Ibn 'Asakir presents Ash'ari's middle path on ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari navigates between anthropomorphism and denial by affirming the vision of God without modality or limitation.

Core Idea
God is seen without indwelling, limits, or how.

The question of whether believers will see God in the afterlife divided early Islamic sects. Some insisted on a physical, limited vision, while others denied it entirely. Imam al-Ash'ari charted a middle course: the vision is real, but it is not like any created vision. This passage from Ibn 'Asakir presents that balanced position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two extremes
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Ibn 'Asakir first mentions the Hashwiyya anthropomorphists who claimed a limited vision, then the Mu'tazila and others who denied vision entirely.
These extremes set the stage for Ash'ari's middle path.
2
Analyze Ash'ari's formulation
Focus on the phrase 'without indwelling, without limits, and without modality'. Compare it with how God sees us—without being limited. This analogy is key.
Ash'ari affirms the reality of vision but purifies it of any creaturely attributes.
3
Reflect on the principle of tanzih
This position upholds divine transcendence while accepting the literal promise of ru'ya in the Quran and hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتوسط الأشعري في رؤية الباري، فيقول ابن عساكر: وكذلك قالت الحشوية المشبهة: إن الله سبحانه وتعالى يرى مكيفاً محدوداً كسائر المرئيات. وقالت المعتزلة والجهمية والنجارية: إنه سبحانه لا يرى بحال. فسلك طريقة بينهما فقال: يرى من غير حلول ولا حدود ولا تكييف، كما يرانا هو سبحانه وتعالى وهو غير محدود ولا مكيف، فكذلك نراه، وهو غير محدود ولا مكيف. وإلى مثل ذلك أيضاً يذهب الماتريدي، فنقرأ في كتاب التوحيد: القول في رؤية الرب عز وجل عندنا لازم وحق من غير إدراك ولا تفسير... ولا نقول بالإدراك لقوله: لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ فقد امتدح به بنفي الإدراك لا بنفي الرؤية. وأيضاً إن الإدراك إنما هو الإحاطة بالمحدود، والله يتعالى عن وصف الحد. فإن قيل: كيف يرى؟ قيل: بلا كيف؛ إذ الكيفية تكون لذي صورة، بل يرى بلا وصف قيام وقعود، واتكاء وتعلق، واتصال وانفصال، ومقابلة ومدابرة، وقصير وطويل، ونور وظلمة، وساكن ومتحرك، ومماس ومباين، وخارج وداخل، ولا معنى يأخذه الوهم أو يقدره العقل.
And al-Ash'ari takes the middle position regarding the vision of the Creator. Ibn 'Asakir says: Likewise the Hashwiyya anthropomorphists said that God, glorified and exalted, is seen as qualified and limited like other visible things. And the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Najjariyya said that He, glorified, is not seen at all. So he followed a path between them and said: He is seen without indwelling, without limits, and without modality, just as He sees us, glorified and exalted, while He is not limited and not qualified, so we see Him, and He is not limited and not qualified. And al-Maturidi also goes to a similar position. We read in the Kitab al-Tawhid: The statement concerning the vision of the Lord, mighty and majestic, is for us necessary and true, without comprehension (idrak) and without interpretation... And we do not say comprehension because of His saying: 'Eyes comprehend Him not' (6:103) – He praised Himself by negating comprehension, not by negating vision. And also comprehension is only the encompassing of the limited, and God is exalted beyond the description of limit. If it is said: How is He seen? It is said: Without how; for modality pertains to one possessing form. Rather, He is seen without description of standing or sitting, leaning or attachment, connection or separation, encountering or withdrawing, short or long, light or darkness, motionless or moving, touching or separate, outside or inside, and without any meaning that imagination can seize or the intellect can estimate.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage presents the middle position of Ash'ari and Maturidi on the vision of God (ru'ya). It begins by stating two extreme views: the Hashwiyya anthropomorphists claim God is seen with modality and limitation like creatures; the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Najjariyya deny vision entirely. Ash'ari takes the middle path: God is seen but without indwelling, limits, or modality—analogous to how He sees us without being limited. Maturidi, quoting his Kitab al-Tawhid, agrees and elaborates. He affirms ru'ya as necessary and true, but denies idrak (comprehension), citing the Qur'anic verse 'Eyes comprehend Him not' to argue that the negation is of comprehension, not vision. He explains that idrak means encompassing a limited object, and God is beyond any limit. When asked 'how?', the answer is 'without how' (bila kayf) because modality is for beings with form. He then lists a series of human attributes (standing, sitting, etc.) that do not apply to God's vision. The passage emphasizes transcendence (tanzih) while affirming the reality of vision as promised in the afterlife.

Key Terms
رؤية
كيف
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming ru'ya means God is seen in a physical form.
Correction: Ash'ari affirms vision without limits or modality, so it is not like human vision.

Checkpoint
What does Ash'ari negate in his formulation of ru'ya?
A) The reality of vision
B) Indwelling, limits, and modality
C) The ability to see God
D) The Quranic verse

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari affirms the vision of God while upholding divine transcendence by negating any creaturely attributes in that vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine seeing Allah in the afterlife, do you catch yourself picturing something with limits or shape, even slightly? How does Ash'ari's negation of kayfiyya challenge your mental image, and what can you do now to purify your concept of Allah from any trace of imagination?
?
The analogy 'as He sees us' is meant to affirm vision without attachment. But do you truly live as if Allah sees you every moment—without limits, without distance, without any veil? If that reality settled in your heart, what would change in your actions today?
?
You have just learned that the middle path on ru'ya avoids both denial and anthropomorphism. But what is your default tendency: do you sometimes feel far from Allah because you think you cannot 'see' or feel Him, or do you casually imagine Him as close in a human-like way? Which extreme do you lean toward, and what is one concrete step you will take to walk the middle path in your worship?

Maturidi's Affirmation of Ru'ya without Idrak
Maturidi affirms ru'ya while denying idrak, using Quranic proof and tanzih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that vision of God (ru'ya) is true but without comprehension (idrak), and why 'how' is inapplicable.

Core Idea
We see God without comprehending Him.

Imam al-Maturidi, like Ash'ari, affirms the vision of God but carefully distinguishes vision (ru'ya) from comprehension (idrak). He draws on the Quranic verse 'Eyes comprehend Him not' to argue that negation applies only to encompassing, not to sight itself. This passage from his Kitab al-Tawhid explains why the question 'how' is invalid for God's vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and find Maturidi's key distinction
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Maturidi begins by affirming ru'ya as necessary and true, then immediately negates idrak and tafsir (interpretation).
The distinction between ru'ya and idrak is central.
2
Analyze the proof from the Quran
Maturidi cites verse 6:103. He argues that 'idrak' means 'encompassing a limited object'. Since God is not limited, the verse negates idrak, not ru'ya.
3
Understand the rejection of 'how'
Maturidi answers the question 'How is He seen?' with 'Without how', because modality pertains to form. He then lists dozens of creaturely attributes that do not apply.
The list emphasizes tanzih (transcendence).
4
Reflect on the scope
This passage shows that Maturidi's position is not a compromise but a precise theological formulation.

Source Text

The Middle Path on the Throne and Istawa'
Ash'ari and Maturidi affirm istiwa' without modality or location, rejecting God being in a place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the middle path of Ash'ari and Maturidi regarding God's relationship to the Throne—affirming istiwa' without spatial location or modality.

Core Idea
God was without place, created the Throne, and remains transcendent of location.

This lesson presents two extreme views on God's relation to the Throne—the Najjariyya who say God is everywhere without location, and the Hashwiyya who claim He is literally seated. Between them, Ash'ari and Maturidi chart a middle path: affirming that God existed without place, created the Throne without need, and remains as He was before creation. The passage establishes that divine transcendence is preserved while affirming scriptural descriptions of istiwa' and the Throne.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two extreme views
Read the passage and locate the positions of the Najjariyya and the Hashwiyya. Notice how each side misinterprets divine transcendence.
The Najjariyya deny any location; the Hashwiyya limit God to a place.
2
Find Ash'ari's via media
Look for the sentence beginning with 'فقال: كان ولا مكان'. This is the core of the middle path: God exists without place, creates the Throne without need, and remains unchanged.
The key phrase is 'هو بعد خلق المكان كما كان قبل خلقه'.
3
See how Maturidi concurs
Read the portion from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid. He reports the disagreement and shows that the Hashwiyya appeal to verses about angels carrying the Throne.
Maturidi does not endorse the Hashwiyya view but presents it as one opinion.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why this middle path is important: it affirms the Qur'anic mention of the Throne and istiwa' without compromising God's transcendence.
The Throne is not a spatial container for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتوسط الأشعري في بيان العرش والاستواء، فيقول ابن عساكر: وكذلك قالت النجارية: إن الباري سبحانه بكل مكان من غير حلول ولا جهة، وقالت الحشوية المجسمة: إنه سبحانه حال في العرش، وأن العرش مكان له، وهو جالس عليه. فسلك طريقة بينها فقال: كان ولا مكان، فخلق العرش والكرسي ولم يحتج إلى مكان، وهو بعد خلق المكان كما كان قبل خلقه. وإلى مثل ذلك أيضاً يذهب الماتريدي، فنقرأ من كتاب التوحيد: ثم اختلف أهل الإسلام في القول بالمكان، فمنهم من زعم أنه يوصف بأنه على العرش والعرش عندهم السرير المحمول بالملائكة المحفوف: وَيَحْمِلُ عَرْشَ رَبِّكَ فَوْقَهُمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ ثَمَانِيَةٌ، وقوله: وَتَرَى المَلائِكَةَ حَافِّينَ مِنْ حَوْلِ العرش، وقوله: الَّذِينَ يَحْمِلُونَ العَرْشَ وَمَنْ حَوْلَه. واحتجوا للقول به
And al-Ash'ari takes the middle position in clarifying the Throne and the Istawa'. Ibn 'Asakir says: Likewise the Najjariyya said that the Creator, glorified, is in every place without indwelling or direction, and the Hashwiyya anthropomorphists said that He, glorified, is indwelling in the Throne, and that the Throne is a place for Him, and He is seated upon it. So he followed a path between them and said: He was and there was no place. Then He created the Throne and the Kursi and had no need of a place, and He is after the creation of place as He was before its creation. And al-Maturidi also goes to a similar position. We read in the Kitab al-Tawhid: Then the people of Islam differed concerning the statement about place. Some of them claimed that He is described as being upon the Throne, and the Throne with them is the bed carried by the surrounding angels: 'And eight will carry the Throne of your Lord above them on that Day' (69:17), and His saying: 'And you will see the angels surrounding the Throne' (39:75), and His saying: 'Those who carry the Throne and those around it' (40:7). And they argued for the statement about it...
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage addresses the theological debate over God's relationship to the Throne (al-'arsh) and His attribute of istiwa' (establishing Himself over it). It presents two extreme views: the Najjariyya say God is everywhere without indwelling or direction; the Hashwiyya anthropomorphists say God is literally seated on the Throne as a place. Ash'ari takes the middle path: God was without place before creation; He created the Throne and Kursi without needing them; and after creation He remains as He was—transcendent of place. Maturidi agrees, quoting his Kitab al-Tawhid. He reports the disagreement among Muslims about place. Some (the Hashwiyya) claim God is upon the Throne, interpreting 'Throne' as a physical bed carried by angels, citing verses about angels carrying the Throne. Maturidi does not endorse their view but presents it as one position. The passage emphasizes divine transcendence: God is not confined to any location, and affirmations of istiwa' must be understood without modality or corporeality.

استواء
مكان
جهة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming istiwa' means God is physically seated or located in the Throne.
Correction: The middle path affirms istiwa' without modality or spatial limitation; God is not confined to place.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position affirms the Throne and istiwa' while maintaining that God is transcendent, not confined to any place or direction.
